## Chapter: Basic Concepts of Igneous Petrology

### Introduction

Welcome to Chapter 3 of "Advanced Igneous Petrology Textbook". In this chapter, we will delve into the basic concepts of igneous petrology, providing a solid foundation for the more advanced topics covered in the subsequent chapters. 

Igneous petrology is a fascinating field that studies the origin, composition, and evolution of igneous rocks. These rocks are formed from molten rock, or magma, which solidifies and cools over time. Understanding the basic concepts of igneous petrology is crucial for comprehending the complex processes that shape the Earth's crust and mantle.

In this chapter, we will explore the fundamental principles that govern the formation and evolution of igneous rocks. We will discuss the different types of igneous rocks, their characteristics, and how they are formed. We will also delve into the processes that control the composition of magma, including the role of volatiles and the influence of the surrounding environment.

We will also introduce the concept of petrologic classification, a system used to categorize igneous rocks based on their mineral composition and texture. This classification system is essential for understanding the origin and evolution of igneous rocks, as different types of rocks have different origins and histories.

By the end of this chapter, you should have a solid understanding of the basic concepts of igneous petrology, providing a strong foundation for the more advanced topics covered in the subsequent chapters. So, let's embark on this exciting journey into the world of igneous petrology.




### Subsection: 2.7a Processes and consequences of magma mixing

Magma mixing is a crucial process in the formation of igneous rocks. It involves the blending of different magmas, each with its own unique composition and characteristics. This process can have significant implications for the resulting rock, including its mineralogy, texture, and chemical composition.

#### 2.7a.1 Mechanisms of Magma Mixing

There are several mechanisms by which magma mixing can occur. One common mechanism is through the mixing of different magma chambers. As magma rises from the mantle, it can encounter other magmas with different compositions, leading to mixing. This can occur at various depths, from the upper mantle to the shallow crust.

Another mechanism is through the mixing of magmas with different compositions as they erupt. This can occur at volcanic vents, where different magmas can mix and create new compositions. This process can also occur during explosive volcanic eruptions, where magma fragments can mix with other magmas and form new compositions.

#### 2.7a.2 Consequences of Magma Mixing

The consequences of magma mixing can be significant. One of the most notable consequences is the creation of new mineral assemblages. As different magmas mix, they can create new minerals that are not present in either of the original magmas. This can lead to the formation of unique and complex mineral assemblages in the resulting rock.

Magma mixing can also result in changes in the chemical composition of the resulting rock. As different magmas mix, they can exchange elements and ions, leading to changes in the overall composition of the rock. This can result in the formation of new minerals and textures, as well as changes in the physical properties of the rock.

#### 2.7a.3 Magma Mixing and Crustal Assimilation

In addition to mixing, magma can also undergo assimilation of crustal rocks. This process involves the incorporation of crustal rocks into the magma, which can significantly alter its composition. Crustal assimilation can occur through various mechanisms, including the mixing of magma with hydrothermal fluids and the incorporation of crustal rocks into the magma chamber.

Crustal assimilation can have significant implications for the resulting rock. It can lead to the formation of new minerals and textures, as well as changes in the chemical composition of the rock. This process can also result in the formation of new igneous rocks, such as granite, which is a common product of crustal assimilation.

### Conclusion

Magma mixing and crustal assimilation are crucial processes in the formation of igneous rocks. They can result in the creation of new mineral assemblages, changes in the chemical composition of the rock, and the formation of new igneous rocks. Understanding these processes is essential for the study of igneous petrology and the interpretation of geological phenomena.





### Subsection: 2.7b Crustal assimilation in igneous systems

Crustal assimilation is a crucial process in the formation of igneous rocks. It involves the incorporation of crustal rocks into the magma, which can significantly alter the composition and properties of the resulting rock. This process can occur through various mechanisms, including magma mixing and meteoric water interaction.

#### 2.7b.1 Mechanisms of Crustal Assimilation

One of the primary mechanisms of crustal assimilation is through magma mixing. As discussed in the previous section, magma mixing can occur at various depths and can result in the incorporation of crustal rocks into the magma. This can occur through the mixing of different magmas or through the mixing of magma with crustal rocks as it erupts.

Another mechanism of crustal assimilation is through meteoric water interaction. Meteoric water, or water that has interacted with the Earth's crust, can enter the magma system and interact with the magma. This interaction can result in the incorporation of crustal rocks into the magma, as well as changes in the chemical composition of the magma.

#### 2.7b.2 Consequences of Crustal Assimilation

The consequences of crustal assimilation can be significant. One of the most notable consequences is the formation of new minerals. As crustal rocks are incorporated into the magma, they can contribute new minerals to the resulting rock. This can result in the formation of unique and complex mineral assemblages, as well as changes in the physical properties of the rock.

Crustal assimilation can also result in changes in the chemical composition of the resulting rock. As crustal rocks are incorporated into the magma, they can contribute new elements and ions, leading to changes in the overall composition of the rock. This can result in the formation of new minerals and textures, as well as changes in the physical properties of the rock.

#### 2.7b.3 Crustal Assimilation and Magma Mixing

Crustal assimilation and magma mixing are closely intertwined processes. Magma mixing can facilitate crustal assimilation by allowing for the incorporation of crustal rocks into the magma. Similarly, crustal assimilation can enhance magma mixing by altering the composition and properties of the magma.

The combination of these two processes can result in the formation of complex and diverse igneous rocks. The incorporation of crustal rocks into the magma can lead to the formation of new minerals and textures, while the mixing of different magmas can result in changes in the chemical composition of the rock. This can create a wide range of igneous rocks with unique properties and applications.

In the next section, we will explore the role of crustal assimilation and magma mixing in the formation of specific types of igneous rocks.


### Conclusion
In this chapter, we have explored the fundamental concepts of igneous petrology, including the classification, chemical variations, and graphical representations of igneous rocks. We have learned about the different types of igneous rocks, their characteristics, and how they are formed. We have also delved into the chemical variations of these rocks, understanding how they are influenced by factors such as temperature, pressure, and the composition of the parental magma. Furthermore, we have discussed the graphical representations of igneous rocks, including the use of phase diagrams and mineral assemblage diagrams to understand the evolution of these rocks.

Through this chapter, we have gained a deeper understanding of the complex world of igneous petrology. We have learned about the intricate processes that govern the formation of igneous rocks and how they can be classified and represented graphically. This knowledge is crucial for any geologist or petrologist, as it allows us to better understand the Earth's interior and the processes that shape it.

Metamorphic rocks are formed when igneous or sedimentary rocks are subjected to high temperatures and pressures, resulting in changes in their mineral composition and texture. This process is known as metamorphism and it plays a crucial role in the evolution of the Earth's crust. 

We will begin by exploring the different types of metamorphic rocks and their characteristics. We will then discuss the various factors that influence metamorphism, such as temperature, pressure, and the composition of the parent rock. We will also examine the different types of metamorphic reactions that occur during the process. 

Next, we will delve into the concept of metamorphic facies, which are groups of metamorphic rocks that share similar mineral assemblages and textures. We will discuss the different metamorphic facies and their significance in understanding the history of the Earth's crust. 

Finally, we will explore the role of metamorphic rocks in the formation of ore deposits. Many valuable minerals and metals are found in metamorphic rocks, and we will discuss the processes that lead to their formation. 

By the end of this chapter, you will have a comprehensive understanding of metamorphic petrology and its importance in the study of the Earth's crust. So let's dive in and explore the fascinating world of metamorphic rocks.


# Advanced Igneous Petrology Textbook

## Chapter 3: Metamorphic Petrology

 3.1: Metamorphic Rocks

Metamorphic rocks are a fascinating and diverse group of rocks that are formed through the process of metamorphism. Metamorphism is the transformation of one rock type into another due to changes in temperature, pressure, and chemical reactions. In this section, we will explore the different types of metamorphic rocks and their characteristics.

#### 3.1a: Types of Metamorphic Rocks

There are three main types of metamorphic rocks: low-grade, high-grade, and ultra-high-grade metamorphic rocks. Low-grade metamorphic rocks are formed at temperatures below 400°C and pressures below 1 kilobar. High-grade metamorphic rocks are formed at temperatures above 400°C and pressures above 1 kilobar. Ultra-high-grade metamorphic rocks are formed at temperatures above 600°C and pressures above 2 kilobars.

Low-grade metamorphic rocks are further divided into greenschist, chlorite, and albite facies. These rocks are characterized by the presence of green minerals, such as chlorite and actinolite, and have a relatively low metamorphic grade. High-grade metamorphic rocks are divided into amphibolite, biotite, and sillimanite facies. These rocks are characterized by the presence of dark minerals, such as amphibole and biotite, and have a higher metamorphic grade than low-grade rocks. Ultra-high-grade metamorphic rocks are divided into eclogite and granulite facies. These rocks are characterized by the presence of high-pressure minerals, such as omphacite and rutile, and have the highest metamorphic grade of all metamorphic rocks.

Each type of metamorphic rock has its own unique mineral assemblage and texture, which can provide valuable information about the conditions under which it was formed. For example, the presence of certain minerals, such as sillimanite or biotite, can indicate the temperature and pressure at which the rock was metamorphosed.

In addition to their mineral composition, metamorphic rocks also exhibit different textures depending on the type of parent rock and the metamorphic conditions. For instance, low-grade metamorphic rocks tend to have a fine-grained texture, while high-grade rocks have a coarser texture due to the recrystallization of minerals at higher temperatures and pressures.

Overall, the study of metamorphic rocks is crucial in understanding the evolution of the Earth's crust. By examining the mineral composition and texture of these rocks, we can gain insights into the processes that have shaped our planet over billions of years. In the next section, we will delve deeper into the factors that influence metamorphism and the different types of metamorphic reactions that occur during the process.


# Advanced Igneous Petrology Textbook

## Chapter 3: Metamorphic Petrology

 3.1: Metamorphic Rocks

Metamorphic rocks are a fascinating and diverse group of rocks that are formed through the process of metamorphism. Metamorphism is the transformation of one rock type into another due to changes in temperature, pressure, and chemical reactions. In this section, we will explore the different types of metamorphic rocks and their characteristics.

#### 3.1a: Types of Metamorphic Rocks

There are three main types of metamorphic rocks: low-grade, high-grade, and ultra-high-grade metamorphic rocks. Low-grade metamorphic rocks are formed at temperatures below 400°C and pressures below 1 kilobar. High-grade metamorphic rocks are formed at temperatures above 400°C and pressures above 1 kilobar. Ultra-high-grade metamorphic rocks are formed at temperatures above 600°C and pressures above 2 kilobars.

Low-grade metamorphic rocks are further divided into greenschist, chlorite, and albite facies. These rocks are characterized by the presence of green minerals, such as chlorite and actinolite, and have a relatively low metamorphic grade. High-grade metamorphic rocks are divided into amphibolite, biotite, and sillimanite facies. These rocks are characterized by the presence of dark minerals, such as amphibole and biotite, and have a higher metamorphic grade than low-grade rocks. Ultra-high-grade metamorphic rocks are divided into eclogite and granulite facies. These rocks are characterized by the presence of high-pressure minerals, such as omphacite and rutile, and have the highest metamorphic grade of all metamorphic rocks.

Each type of metamorphic rock has its own unique mineral assemblage and texture, which can provide valuable information about the conditions under which it was formed. For example, the presence of certain minerals, such as sillimanite or biotite, can indicate the temperature and pressure at which the rock was metamorphosed. Additionally, the texture of the rock can also provide clues about the deformation and recrystallization processes that occurred during metamorphism.

### Subsection: 3.1b Metamorphic Processes

Metamorphic processes are the mechanisms by which metamorphic rocks are formed. These processes can be broadly categorized into two types: contact metamorphism and regional metamorphism.

Contact metamorphism occurs when a rock is heated by a nearby intrusive igneous body, such as a pluton or a sill. The heat from the intrusive body causes the surrounding rock to undergo metamorphism, resulting in the formation of a metamorphic aureole. The size and shape of the aureole depend on the size and composition of the intrusive body, as well as the distance from the intrusion.

Regional metamorphism, on the other hand, occurs when a large area of rock is subjected to high temperatures and pressures. This can happen due to plate movements, such as subduction or collision, or due to the presence of a deep magma chamber. Regional metamorphism can result in the formation of large-scale metamorphic structures, such as gneiss domes or metamorphic shear zones.

Both contact and regional metamorphism can result in the formation of different types of metamorphic rocks, depending on the specific conditions under which they occur. For example, contact metamorphism is more likely to result in the formation of high-grade metamorphic rocks, while regional metamorphism can produce both high-grade and low-grade rocks.

In addition to these two main types of metamorphism, there are also other processes that can contribute to the formation of metamorphic rocks, such as hydrothermal alteration and fluid-rock interactions. These processes can further complicate the mineral assemblage and texture of metamorphic rocks, making them even more interesting to study.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the specific metamorphic processes and their effects on the formation of metamorphic rocks. 


# Advanced Igneous Petrology Textbook

## Chapter 3: Metamorphic Petrology

High-grade metamorphic rocks are divided into amphibolite, biotite, and sillimanite facies. These rocks are characterized by the presence of dark minerals, such as amphibole and biotite, and have a higher metamorphic grade than low-grade rocks. Ultra-high-grade metamorphic rocks are divided into eclogite and granulite facies. These rocks are characterized by the presence of high-pressure minerals, such as omphacite and rutile, and have the highest metamorphic grade of all metamorphic rocks.

Each type of metamorphic rock has its own unique mineral assemblage and texture, which can provide valuable information about the conditions under which it was formed. For example, the presence of certain minerals, such as sillimanite or biotite, can indicate the temperature and pressure at which the rock was metamorphosed. Additionally, the texture of the rock can also provide clues about the deformation and recrystallization processes that occurred during metamorphism.

### Subsection: 3.1b Metamorphic Processes

Metamorphic processes are the mechanisms by which metamorphic rocks are formed. These processes can be broadly categorized into two types: contact metamorphism and regional metamorphism.

Contact metamorphism occurs when a rock is heated by a nearby intrusive igneous body, such as a pluton or a sill. The heat from the intrusive body causes the surrounding rock to undergo metamorphism, resulting in the formation of a metamorphic aureole. The size and shape of the aureole depend on the size and composition of the intrusive body, as well as the distance from the intrusion.

Regional metamorphism, on the other hand, occurs when a large area of rock is subjected to high temperatures and pressures. This can happen due to plate movements, such as subduction or collision, or due to the presence of a deep magma chamber. Regional metamorphism can result in the formation of large-scale metamorphic structures, such as gneiss domes or metamorphic shear zones.

Both contact and regional metamorphism can result in the formation of different types of metamorphic rocks, depending on the specific conditions under which they occur. For example, contact metamorphism is more likely to result in the formation of high-grade metamorphic rocks, while regional metamorphism can produce both high-grade and low-grade rocks.

### Subsection: 3.1c Metamorphic Facies

Metamorphic facies are groups of metamorphic rocks that share similar mineral assemblages and textures. These facies are classified based on the dominant mineral assemblage and the metamorphic grade of the rocks. The three main metamorphic facies are the greenschist facies, the amphibolite facies, and the eclogite facies.

The greenschist facies is characterized by the presence of green minerals, such as chlorite and actinolite, and has a relatively low metamorphic grade. This facies is commonly found in low-grade metamorphic rocks and is often associated with hydrothermal alteration.

The amphibolite facies is characterized by the presence of dark minerals, such as amphibole and biotite, and has a higher metamorphic grade than the greenschist facies. This facies is commonly found in high-grade metamorphic rocks and is often associated with regional metamorphism.

The eclogite facies is characterized by the presence of high-pressure minerals, such as omphacite and rutile, and has the highest metamorphic grade of all metamorphic rocks. This facies is commonly found in ultra-high-grade metamorphic rocks and is often associated with deep subduction zones.

Understanding the different metamorphic facies can provide valuable information about the conditions under which a rock was metamorphosed. For example, the presence of certain minerals, such as sillimanite or biotite, can indicate the temperature and pressure at which the rock was metamorphosed. Additionally, the texture of the rock can also provide clues about the deformation and recrystallization processes that occurred during metamorphism.


# Advanced Igneous Petrology Textbook

## Chapter 3: Metamorphic Petrology




### Subsection: 2.8a Petrologic and geochemical features of mafic igneous systems

Mafic igneous systems are characterized by their high magnesium and iron content, as well as their low silicon content. These rocks are typically formed through the crystallization of basaltic magmas, which are derived from the mantle. The composition of mafic igneous systems can vary greatly, depending on the source of the magma and the processes it undergoes during its formation and evolution.

#### 2.8a.1 Petrologic Features of Mafic Igneous Systems

The petrologic features of mafic igneous systems are largely determined by the composition of the magma from which they are formed. As mentioned earlier, basaltic magmas are typically high in magnesium and iron, and low in silicon. This results in the formation of minerals such as olivine, pyroxene, and plagioclase feldspar.

The crystallization of these minerals can occur in a specific order, known as the Bowen's reaction series. This series describes the order in which minerals crystallize from a cooling magma, with olivine and pyroxene crystallizing first, followed by plagioclase feldspar. This series can be disrupted by various factors, such as changes in temperature or pressure, or the presence of foreign elements.

#### 2.8a.2 Geochemical Features of Mafic Igneous Systems

The geochemical features of mafic igneous systems are also determined by the composition of the magma. The high magnesium and iron content of these rocks can result in high concentrations of these elements in the surrounding environment. This can have significant impacts on the surrounding ecosystem, particularly in areas where these rocks are being actively extruded, such as mid-ocean ridges.

The geochemical features of mafic igneous systems can also provide valuable insights into the processes they undergo during their formation and evolution. For example, the presence of certain elements or isotopes can indicate the source of the magma, whether it is from the mantle or from the crust. This can help to shed light on the tectonic history of the region.

#### 2.8a.3 Graphical Representations of Mafic Igneous Systems

Graphical representations, such as phase diagrams and mineral assemblage diagrams, can be useful tools for understanding the petrologic and geochemical features of mafic igneous systems. These diagrams can help to illustrate the crystallization of minerals and the changes in composition that occur during the formation and evolution of these rocks.

For example, a phase diagram can show the temperature and pressure conditions at which different minerals crystallize from a magma. This can help to explain why certain minerals are more common in some mafic igneous systems than others. Similarly, a mineral assemblage diagram can show the composition of a rock at different stages of its crystallization, providing insights into the processes that occur during its formation.

In conclusion, the petrologic and geochemical features of mafic igneous systems are largely determined by the composition of the magma from which they are formed. These features can be further understood through the use of graphical representations, such as phase diagrams and mineral assemblage diagrams. By studying these features, we can gain a deeper understanding of the processes that shape our planet's crust.

We have learned about the different types of igneous rocks, including plutonic and volcanic rocks, and how they are formed. Furthermore, we have discussed the graphical representations of igneous rocks, including the use of phase diagrams and mineral assemblage diagrams.

Through this chapter, we have gained a deeper understanding of the complex world of igneous petrology. We have also explored the chemical variations of these rocks, understanding the significance of major and trace elements in their composition. ## Chapter: Advanced Igneous Petrology Textbook

### Introduction

In this chapter, we will delve into the topic of metamorphic petrology, which is the study of the changes that occur in rocks due to high temperatures and pressures. Metamorphic rocks are formed when igneous or sedimentary rocks are subjected to extreme conditions, such as high temperatures and pressures, or exposure to hot fluids. These changes can result in the formation of new minerals and textures, as well as alterations in the chemical composition of the original rock. Understanding metamorphic petrology is crucial for understanding the evolution of the Earth's crust and the processes that shape it.

Throughout this chapter, we will explore the various types of metamorphic rocks, their formation processes, and the factors that influence their development. We will also discuss the different types of metamorphic facies, which are distinct assemblages of minerals that form under specific conditions. Additionally, we will examine the role of metamorphic rocks in the formation of ore deposits and their importance in mineral exploration.

By the end of this chapter, readers will have a comprehensive understanding of metamorphic petrology and its significance in the field of geology. This knowledge will not only enhance their understanding of the Earth's crust and its evolution, but also provide them with the necessary tools to identify and interpret metamorphic rocks in the field. So let us begin our journey into the fascinating world of metamorphic petrology.


# Advanced Igneous Petrology Textbook

## Chapter 3: Metamorphic Petrology

 3.1: Metamorphic Facies

Metamorphic facies are distinct assemblages of minerals that form under specific conditions of temperature, pressure, and fluid chemistry. These conditions are often extreme, and can result in the formation of new minerals and textures, as well as alterations in the chemical composition of the original rock. In this section, we will explore the different types of metamorphic facies and their characteristics.

#### 3.1a: Definition and Classification of Metamorphic Facies

Metamorphic facies can be defined as the assemblage of minerals that form under specific conditions of temperature, pressure, and fluid chemistry. These conditions can vary greatly, and as a result, there are several different types of metamorphic facies that have been identified. These include:

- Eclogite facies: This facies is characterized by high pressures and temperatures, and is typically found in the deep mantle or in subduction zones. The dominant minerals in this facies are olivine, omphacite, and rutile.
- Greenschist facies: This facies is found at lower pressures and temperatures than the eclogite facies, and is commonly found in the upper mantle or in the vicinity of mid-ocean ridges. The dominant minerals in this facies are chlorite, albite, and hornblende.
- Amphibolite facies: This facies is found at even lower pressures and temperatures than the greenschist facies, and is commonly found in the lower crust or in the vicinity of mountain ranges. The dominant minerals in this facies are plagioclase feldspar, hornblende, and biotite.
- Sillimanite facies: This facies is found at high temperatures and pressures, and is commonly found in the lower crust or in the vicinity of mountain ranges. The dominant minerals in this facies are sillimanite, muscovite, and biotite.
- Quartzite facies: This facies is found at high temperatures and pressures, and is commonly found in the upper crust or in the vicinity of mountain ranges. The dominant minerals in this facies are quartz, muscovite, and biotite.

These facies are not mutually exclusive, and a single rock can exhibit characteristics of multiple facies. The dominant facies of a rock is determined by the conditions it experiences during metamorphism.

In addition to these facies, there are also several other types of metamorphic rocks that do not fit into the above categories. These include:

- Skarn: This type of rock is formed when a sedimentary or igneous rock is exposed to hydrothermal fluids that contain high concentrations of metals. The dominant minerals in skarn are sulfides, oxides, and silicates.
- Bauxite: This type of rock is formed when a sedimentary or igneous rock is exposed to hydrothermal fluids that contain high concentrations of aluminum. The dominant minerals in bauxite are kaolinite, gibbsite, and boehmite.
- Serpentinite: This type of rock is formed when a peridotite is exposed to hydrothermal fluids that contain high concentrations of magnesium. The dominant mineral in serpentinite is serpentine.

Understanding the different types of metamorphic facies and their characteristics is crucial for interpreting the formation processes of metamorphic rocks. In the next section, we will explore the factors that influence the development of metamorphic facies.

### Subsection: 2.8b Mantle sources and processes of mafic magmas

The mantle is a crucial source of mafic magmas, which are the primary constituents of mafic igneous systems. The mantle is a layer of the Earth's interior that lies between the crust and the core. It is composed of solid rock, but it can also contain pockets of molten rock, or magma, which can rise to the surface and form mafic igneous systems.

#### 2.8b.1 Mantle Sources of Mafic Magmas

The mantle is a complex and dynamic system, with various sources of mafic magmas. These sources can be broadly categorized into two types: the upper mantle and the lower mantle.

The upper mantle, which extends from a depth of approximately 100 km to 660 km, is the primary source of mafic magmas. It is composed of peridotite, a rock that is rich in iron and magnesium. The upper mantle is also where the process of mantle convection occurs, which is responsible for the movement of heat and material within the mantle. This convection can lead to the formation of plumes, which are columns of hot material that rise from the lower mantle to the upper mantle.

The lower mantle, which extends from a depth of approximately 660 km to the core-mantle boundary at 2,900 km, is also a source of mafic magmas. The lower mantle is composed of a denser and hotter material than the upper mantle, and it is thought to be the source of heat and material for the upper mantle. The lower mantle is also where the process of mantle plumes occurs, which can lead to the formation of hotspots and the creation of mafic igneous systems.

#### 2.8b.2 Processes of Mafic Magma Formation

The formation of mafic magmas is a complex process that involves the melting of the mantle. This melting can occur through various processes, including adiabatic heating, where the mantle material is heated as it rises through the mantle, and thermal metamorphism, where the mantle material is heated by the heat of the Earth's core.

The melting of the mantle can lead to the formation of mafic magmas, which are then transported to the surface through various processes. These processes can include mantle convection, where the magma is transported through the mantle, and mantle plumes, where the magma is transported through the mantle plumes.

The composition of the mafic magmas can vary greatly, depending on the source of the magma and the processes it undergoes during its formation and evolution. For example, the composition of the mafic magmas can be influenced by the composition of the mantle material, the depth at which the magma is formed, and the presence of foreign elements.

In conclusion, the mantle is a crucial source of mafic magmas, and the processes of mantle convection and mantle plumes play a significant role in the formation and evolution of mafic igneous systems. The composition of the mafic magmas can vary greatly, providing valuable insights into the processes they undergo during their formation and evolution.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the fascinating world of advanced igneous petrology, exploring the classification, chemical variations, and graphical representations of igneous rocks. We have learned that igneous rocks are classified based on their mineral composition, texture, and structure, and that they can be broadly categorized into two types: extrusive and intrusive. 

We have also examined the chemical variations in igneous rocks, understanding that these variations are primarily due to the melting of the mantle and the influence of the surrounding environment. We have seen how the chemical composition of igneous rocks can be represented graphically, using tools such as the Q-F-K diagram and the AFM diagram, which provide valuable insights into the processes of igneous rock formation.

In conclusion, advanced igneous petrology is a complex and multifaceted field that requires a deep understanding of geology, chemistry, and mathematics. By studying the classification, chemical variations, and graphical representations of igneous rocks, we can gain a better understanding of the processes that shape our planet's crust and the history of our planet.

## Chapter: Mineral Physics and Experimental Petrology

### Introduction

The study of igneous petrology is a vast and complex field, and one of the most intriguing aspects of this discipline is the exploration of mineral physics and experimental petrology. This chapter will delve into the fascinating world of these two interconnected areas, providing a comprehensive understanding of their role in the broader context of igneous petrology.

Mineral physics is a branch of geophysics that focuses on the physical and chemical properties of minerals, particularly their response to high pressures and temperatures. It is a field that is deeply intertwined with experimental petrology, which is the experimental study of the physical and chemical properties of rocks under various conditions. Together, these two disciplines provide a foundation for understanding the formation and evolution of igneous rocks.

In this chapter, we will explore the principles and techniques used in mineral physics and experimental petrology, including the use of high-pressure and high-temperature experiments to simulate the conditions found in the Earth's mantle and core. We will also discuss the role of these disciplines in the broader context of igneous petrology, including their contributions to our understanding of the formation of igneous rocks and their role in the Earth's geological history.

As we journey through this chapter, we will also touch upon the latest advancements in these fields, including the use of advanced techniques such as synchrotron X-ray diffraction and neutron scattering to study the properties of minerals and rocks. These techniques have revolutionized our understanding of the physical and chemical properties of minerals and rocks, and have opened up new avenues for research in mineral physics and experimental petrology.

This chapter aims to provide a comprehensive overview of mineral physics and experimental petrology, equipping readers with the knowledge and tools necessary to understand and interpret the physical and chemical properties of minerals and rocks. Whether you are a student, a researcher, or a professional in the field of igneous petrology, this chapter will serve as a valuable resource in your exploration of this fascinating field.




### Subsection: 2.9a Effects of water on fractional crystallization

Water plays a crucial role in the process of fractional crystallization, which is a fundamental process in the formation of igneous rocks. The presence of water can significantly alter the crystallization behavior of a magma, leading to changes in the mineralogy and chemical composition of the resulting rocks.

#### 2.9a.1 Water and Crystal Growth

The presence of water in a magma can affect the growth of crystals during fractional crystallization. Water can act as a nucleation agent, promoting the formation of new crystals. This can lead to an increase in the number of crystals and a decrease in the size of the crystals, which can significantly alter the texture of the resulting rock.

Moreover, water can also affect the growth rate of crystals. Studies have shown that the growth rate of crystals decreases linearly with increasing reciprocal concentration, which means that the presence of water can slow down the crystallization process. This can have important implications for the formation of different types of igneous rocks, as the crystallization process can influence the mineralogy and chemical composition of the rock.

#### 2.9a.2 Water and Mineral Stability

Water can also affect the stability of minerals in a magma. The presence of water can alter the solubility of minerals, leading to changes in their stability. This can result in the formation of new minerals or the dissolution of existing minerals, which can significantly alter the mineralogy of the rock.

For example, the presence of water can increase the solubility of minerals such as olivine and pyroxene, leading to their dissolution and the formation of new minerals such as plagioclase feldspar. This can result in the formation of different types of igneous rocks, such as granite, which is primarily composed of plagioclase feldspar.

#### 2.9a.3 Water and Magma Viscosity

Water can also affect the viscosity of a magma, which is a measure of its resistance to flow. The presence of water can decrease the viscosity of a magma, making it more fluid and allowing it to flow more easily. This can have important implications for the formation of different types of igneous rocks, as the viscosity of a magma can influence its ability to flow and the resulting texture of the rock.

In conclusion, the presence of water can significantly alter the crystallization behavior of a magma, leading to changes in the mineralogy and chemical composition of the resulting rocks. Understanding the effects of water on fractional crystallization is crucial for understanding the formation of igneous rocks and their variations.




### Subsection: 2.9b Comparison of wet and dry fractional crystallization processes

Fractional crystallization is a fundamental process in the formation of igneous rocks. It involves the separation of minerals from a melt or solution as it cools and solidifies. This process can occur in both wet and dry conditions, and the differences between these two processes can have significant implications for the resulting rock.

#### 2.9b.1 Wet Fractional Crystallization

Wet fractional crystallization occurs when a melt or solution is in contact with water. The presence of water can significantly alter the crystallization process. As mentioned in the previous section, water can act as a nucleation agent, promoting the formation of new crystals. It can also affect the growth rate of crystals and the stability of minerals.

In wet fractional crystallization, the water can also act as a medium for the transport of ions and minerals. This can lead to the formation of different minerals and the redistribution of elements within the melt. This can result in the formation of more complex and diverse rock types.

#### 2.9b.2 Dry Fractional Crystallization

Dry fractional crystallization, on the other hand, occurs when a melt or solution is not in contact with water. In this process, the crystallization of minerals occurs without the influence of water. This can result in a more straightforward crystallization process, with fewer changes in mineralogy and chemical composition.

However, dry fractional crystallization can also lead to the formation of more homogeneous rock types, as the lack of water can limit the redistribution of elements and minerals. This can result in rocks with more uniform mineralogy and chemical composition.

#### 2.9b.3 Comparison of Wet and Dry Fractional Crystallization

The comparison of wet and dry fractional crystallization processes is complex and depends on a variety of factors, including the composition of the melt or solution, the presence of water, and the temperature and pressure conditions.

In general, wet fractional crystallization can lead to more complex and diverse rock types, due to the influence of water on the crystallization process. However, this can also result in more heterogeneous rock types, with variations in mineralogy and chemical composition.

Dry fractional crystallization, on the other hand, can lead to more homogeneous rock types, with fewer variations in mineralogy and chemical composition. However, this can also result in more straightforward crystallization processes, with fewer changes in mineralogy and chemical composition.

In conclusion, the comparison of wet and dry fractional crystallization processes is complex and depends on a variety of factors. Understanding these differences is crucial for the study of igneous petrology and the formation of different types of rocks.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the fascinating world of igneous petrology, exploring the classification, chemical variations, and graphical representations of igneous rocks. We have learned that igneous rocks are formed from the solidification of molten rock, or magma, and that they can be broadly classified into two types: extrusive and intrusive. Extrusive igneous rocks, such as basalt and andesite, are formed when magma is ejected onto the Earth's surface, while intrusive igneous rocks, such as granite and diorite, are formed when magma cools and solidifies beneath the surface.

We have also examined the chemical variations within igneous rocks, understanding that these variations can be influenced by a variety of factors, including the composition of the original magma, the conditions under which the magma cools and solidifies, and the presence of other minerals or rocks. We have learned that these variations can be represented graphically, using tools such as the QAPF diagram and the AFM diagram, which allow us to visualize the chemical composition of igneous rocks in a clear and intuitive way.

In conclusion, the study of igneous petrology is a complex and rewarding field, offering insights into the processes that shape our planet and the materials that make it up. By understanding the classification, chemical variations, and graphical representations of igneous rocks, we can gain a deeper appreciation for the diversity and beauty of the Earth's crust.

## Chapter 3: The Significance of Igneous Rock Series:

### Introduction

In the previous chapter, we explored the fundamental concepts of igneous petrology, including the formation and classification of igneous rocks. In this chapter, we will delve deeper into the significance of igneous rock series and their role in understanding the evolution of the Earth's crust.

Igneous rock series are a group of related rocks that share similar mineral compositions and textures. They are formed through the crystallization of magma, which is molten rock material. The composition of the magma is influenced by a variety of factors, including the source of the magma, the conditions under which it is formed, and the processes that it undergoes as it cools and solidifies.

The study of igneous rock series is crucial in understanding the processes that shape the Earth's crust. By examining the mineral composition and textures of different igneous rocks, we can gain insights into the conditions under which they were formed and the processes that they have undergone. This information can then be used to reconstruct the history of the Earth's crust and the changes that it has undergone over time.

In this chapter, we will explore the different types of igneous rock series, including their formation, characteristics, and significance. We will also discuss the role of igneous rock series in the larger context of Earth's history and evolution. By the end of this chapter, you will have a deeper understanding of the importance of igneous rock series in the field of igneous petrology.




### Section 3.1 Hydrous Differentiation - The Role of Amphibole:

#### 3.1a Petrology and geochemistry of hydrous differentiation

Hydrous differentiation is a fundamental process in the formation of igneous rocks. It involves the separation of minerals and melts based on their affinity for water. This process is particularly important in the formation of amphibole, a common mineral found in many igneous rocks.

Amphibole is a hydrous silicate mineral that is commonly found in mafic and intermediate igneous rocks. It is characterized by its double chain structure, which allows it to accommodate a significant amount of water. The presence of amphibole in a rock can provide valuable insights into the conditions under which it was formed.

The formation of amphibole is closely tied to the process of hydrous differentiation. As a melt cools and solidifies, minerals with a higher affinity for water will crystallize first, leaving behind a more water-rich melt. This process can lead to the formation of amphibole, which has a high affinity for water.

The geochemistry of amphibole is also important in understanding the process of hydrous differentiation. Amphibole is typically enriched in elements such as iron, magnesium, and calcium, which are commonly found in mafic and intermediate rocks. However, the presence of amphibole can also indicate the presence of water in the melt, as it is one of the few minerals that can accommodate significant amounts of water.

The role of amphibole in hydrous differentiation is crucial in understanding the formation of igneous rocks. By studying the mineral composition and geochemistry of amphibole, we can gain insights into the conditions under which it was formed and the processes that it has undergone. This information can then be used to reconstruct the history of the Earth's crust and the changes that it has undergone over time.

In the next section, we will explore the different types of amphibole and their significance in the formation of igneous rocks. We will also discuss the role of amphibole in the larger context of Earth's history and evolution. By the end of this section, you will have a deeper understanding of the importance of amphibole in the process of hydrous differentiation and its role in the formation of igneous rocks.

We will begin by discussing the basics of metamorphic differentiation, including the different types of metamorphic processes and the factors that influence them. # Advanced Igneous Petrology Textbook

## Chapter 4: Metamorphic Differentiation




### Subsection 3.1b The role of amphibole in hydrous differentiation processes

Amphibole plays a crucial role in the process of hydrous differentiation. As mentioned earlier, amphibole is a hydrous silicate mineral that is commonly found in mafic and intermediate igneous rocks. Its presence can provide valuable insights into the conditions under which a rock was formed.

One of the key roles of amphibole in hydrous differentiation is its ability to accommodate a significant amount of water. As a melt cools and solidifies, minerals with a higher affinity for water will crystallize first, leaving behind a more water-rich melt. Amphibole, with its double chain structure, is one of the minerals that can accommodate this water-rich melt. This allows for the formation of amphibole in the later stages of crystallization, as the melt becomes more water-rich.

The geochemistry of amphibole also plays a crucial role in hydrous differentiation. Amphibole is typically enriched in elements such as iron, magnesium, and calcium, which are commonly found in mafic and intermediate rocks. However, the presence of amphibole can also indicate the presence of water in the melt. This is because amphibole is one of the few minerals that can accommodate significant amounts of water. Therefore, the presence of amphibole can provide evidence for the occurrence of hydrous differentiation.

Furthermore, the role of amphibole in hydrous differentiation can also be seen in its effect on the overall composition of a rock. As amphibole crystallizes, it can incorporate elements from the melt, altering the composition of the melt. This can lead to the formation of different minerals and melts, further contributing to the process of hydrous differentiation.

In conclusion, amphibole plays a crucial role in the process of hydrous differentiation. Its ability to accommodate water and its effect on the overall composition of a rock make it a valuable indicator of the conditions under which a rock was formed. By studying the mineral composition and geochemistry of amphibole, we can gain a deeper understanding of the processes that occur during the formation of igneous rocks.





### Subsection 3.2a Phase equilibria and liquid compositions in hydrous mantle melting

Hydrous mantle melting is a complex process that involves the interaction of various phases and their equilibrium. The composition of the melt plays a crucial role in determining the phase equilibrium and ultimately the composition of the resulting igneous rock. In this section, we will explore the role of phase equilibria and liquid compositions in hydrous mantle melting.

#### Phase Equilibria in Hydrous Mantle Melting

The phase equilibrium in hydrous mantle melting is governed by the principles of thermodynamics. The Gibbs free energy, enthalpy, and entropy of the system all play a role in determining the stability of different phases. The Gibbs free energy is a measure of the energy available to do work in a system at constant temperature and pressure. It is given by the equation:

$$
G = H - TS
$$

where $H$ is the enthalpy, $T$ is the temperature, and $S$ is the entropy. The enthalpy is a measure of the total energy of the system, while the entropy is a measure of the disorder or randomness in the system.

In hydrous mantle melting, the phase equilibrium is determined by the relative values of the Gibbs free energy of the different phases. The phase with the lowest Gibbs free energy is the most stable and will be the dominant phase in the melt. The Gibbs free energy of a phase can be calculated using the equation:

$$
G = H - TS + \mu N
$$

where $\mu$ is the chemical potential and $N$ is the number of moles of the phase. The chemical potential is a measure of the energy required to add or remove a mole of a substance from the system.

#### Liquid Compositions in Hydrous Mantle Melting

The composition of the melt plays a crucial role in determining the phase equilibrium and ultimately the composition of the resulting igneous rock. The composition of the melt is determined by the relative amounts of different phases present in the system. The composition of the melt can be calculated using the equation:

$$
X = \frac{N_1}{N_1 + N_2 + ... + N_n}
$$

where $N_1, N_2, ..., N_n$ are the number of moles of the different phases present in the system. The composition of the melt can also be represented graphically using a ternary diagram, where the composition of the melt is represented by a point within a triangle formed by the three phases.

In hydrous mantle melting, the composition of the melt can be influenced by the presence of water. Water can increase the solubility of certain elements, leading to changes in the composition of the melt. This can result in the formation of different phases and ultimately the formation of different types of igneous rocks.

In conclusion, the phase equilibria and liquid compositions play a crucial role in hydrous mantle melting. The principles of thermodynamics and the composition of the melt determine the phase equilibrium and ultimately the composition of the resulting igneous rock. Understanding these concepts is essential for understanding the significance of igneous rock series.





### Subsection 3.2b Constraints on hydrous mantle melting using experimental and natural data

Hydrous mantle melting is a complex process that is influenced by a variety of factors, including temperature, pressure, and the composition of the mantle. In order to better understand this process, scientists have turned to both experimental and natural data.

#### Experimental Constraints on Hydrous Mantle Melting

Experimental studies have been used to investigate the conditions under which hydrous mantle melting occurs. These studies involve subjecting mantle rocks to high temperatures and pressures, similar to those found in the mantle, and observing the resulting melting behavior.

One of the key findings from these experiments is the role of water in the melting process. Water is a powerful agent for reducing the melting point of mantle rocks, and its presence can significantly increase the amount of melt produced. This is due to the fact that water can weaken the bonds between minerals, making them more susceptible to melting.

Experimental studies have also shed light on the composition of the resulting melt. By analyzing the composition of the melt produced in these experiments, scientists have been able to identify the major and trace elements present in the melt, as well as their relative abundances. This information can then be compared to natural data to further understand the melting process.

#### Natural Constraints on Hydrous Mantle Melting

Natural data, such as the composition of volcanic rocks and the isotopic signature of mantle plumes, can also provide valuable insights into hydrous mantle melting. By studying the composition of volcanic rocks, scientists can determine the relative amounts of different elements and isotopes present in the melt. This information can then be used to infer the composition of the mantle source and the conditions under which the melt was produced.

Isotopic data, in particular, has been used to study the melting process. Isotopes of elements, such as oxygen and hydrogen, can provide information about the source of the water involved in the melting process. For example, the presence of deuterium, a heavy isotope of hydrogen, can indicate the involvement of subducted oceanic crust in the melting process.

In addition to studying the composition of volcanic rocks, scientists have also used seismic data to study the melting process. Seismic tomography, for example, has been used to map the temperature and composition of the mantle, providing valuable insights into the conditions under which hydrous mantle melting occurs.

Overall, both experimental and natural data have been crucial in advancing our understanding of hydrous mantle melting. By combining these two approaches, scientists have been able to gain a more comprehensive understanding of this complex process. 





### Subsection 3.3a Phase relations and petrogenetic processes in felsic magmas

Felsic magmas, also known as silicic magmas, are a type of magma that is rich in silica and have a high viscosity. They are characterized by their high silica content, typically greater than 63%, and are responsible for the formation of many of the world's most well-known volcanic features, such as the Grand Canyon and Yellowstone Caldera.

#### Phase Relations in Felsic Magmas

The phase relations in felsic magmas are complex and highly dependent on the composition of the magma. As mentioned in the previous section, felsic magmas have a high silica content, which means they are dominated by silicate minerals. However, the exact composition of these minerals can vary greatly depending on the source of the magma and the conditions it experiences during its formation and evolution.

One of the key factors that influence the phase relations in felsic magmas is the presence of water. As mentioned in the previous section, water can significantly increase the amount of melt produced during hydrous mantle melting. In felsic magmas, water can also play a crucial role in the formation of different minerals. For example, the presence of water can promote the formation of minerals such as plagioclase feldspar, which is a common mineral in felsic magmas.

#### Petrogenetic Processes in Felsic Magmas

The petrogenetic processes that occur in felsic magmas are also highly dependent on the composition of the magma. As mentioned earlier, felsic magmas have a high silica content, which means they are dominated by silicate minerals. However, the exact composition of these minerals can vary greatly depending on the source of the magma and the conditions it experiences during its formation and evolution.

One of the key processes that occurs in felsic magmas is the crystallization of minerals. As the magma cools and evolves, different minerals will crystallize at different temperatures. This process can be influenced by the presence of water, as well as the composition of the magma. For example, the presence of water can promote the crystallization of minerals such as plagioclase feldspar, while the composition of the magma can influence the crystallization of other minerals such as quartz and biotite.

Another important process that occurs in felsic magmas is the separation of different phases. As the magma evolves, different phases may separate from the melt, forming distinct layers within the magma chamber. This process can be influenced by the composition of the magma, as well as the presence of water. For example, the presence of water can promote the separation of different phases, leading to the formation of distinct layers within the magma chamber.

In conclusion, the phase relations and petrogenetic processes in felsic magmas are highly complex and dependent on the composition of the magma. The presence of water can play a crucial role in these processes, promoting the formation of different minerals and influencing the crystallization and separation of different phases. Further research and study are needed to fully understand these processes and their significance in the formation of felsic magmas.

We have learned about the different types of igneous rocks, their formation processes, and how they are classified based on their composition and texture. We have also discussed the importance of studying igneous rocks in order to gain a deeper understanding of the Earth's history and evolution.

One of the key takeaways from this chapter is the concept of rock series, which refers to a group of related igneous rocks that share similar chemical and mineralogical characteristics. By studying the different rock series, we can gain insights into the processes that have shaped the Earth's crust over time. We have also learned about the different types of igneous rocks, such as basalt, granite, and rhyolite, and how they are formed through different processes, such as extrusive and intrusive igneous activity.

Furthermore, we have discussed the importance of studying the composition and texture of igneous rocks in order to understand their origin and evolution. By analyzing the minerals and elements present in these rocks, we can determine their source and the conditions under which they were formed. This information can then be used to reconstruct the tectonic history of the Earth's crust and the processes that have shaped it.

In conclusion, the study of igneous rock series is crucial in understanding the Earth's crust and its evolution. By studying the different types of igneous rocks and their composition, we can gain a deeper understanding of the processes that have shaped our planet. This knowledge is essential for geologists and other scientists in their efforts to understand the Earth's history and its future.

In this chapter, we will delve into the topic of plutonic rocks, which are a type of igneous rock that is formed deep within the Earth's crust. These rocks are characterized by their coarse-grained texture and their formation through the crystallization of molten rock, or magma. Plutonic rocks are an important aspect of igneous petrology, as they provide valuable insights into the processes and conditions that occur deep within the Earth's crust.

Throughout this chapter, we will explore the various types of plutonic rocks, their formation processes, and their unique characteristics. We will also discuss the role of plutonic rocks in the overall geological history of the Earth, and how they contribute to the formation of other types of rocks and minerals.

By the end of this chapter, readers will have a comprehensive understanding of plutonic rocks and their significance in the field of igneous petrology. This knowledge will not only enhance their understanding of the Earth's crust and its processes, but also provide a solid foundation for further exploration and research in this fascinating field. So let us begin our journey into the world of plutonic rocks and discover the wonders that lie beneath the Earth's surface.


# Advanced Igneous Petrology Textbook

## Chapter 4: Plutonic Rocks

 4.1: Plutonic Textures

Plutonic rocks are a type of igneous rock that is formed deep within the Earth's crust. They are characterized by their coarse-grained texture and their formation through the crystallization of molten rock, or magma. In this section, we will explore the different textures of plutonic rocks and how they are formed.

#### Subsection 4.1a: Coarse-grained Textures in Plutonic Rocks

Coarse-grained textures are a common feature of plutonic rocks. These textures are formed when the magma cools slowly, allowing the minerals to grow and crystallize in a larger size. This results in a coarse-grained texture, with visible grains that are typically larger than 2 millimeters in size.

The formation of coarse-grained textures is influenced by several factors, including the composition of the magma, the temperature and pressure conditions, and the rate of cooling. For example, magmas with a high silica content tend to produce coarse-grained textures, as the silica molecules hinder the growth of minerals and result in larger grains.

Coarse-grained textures are also commonly found in plutonic rocks that have undergone partial melting. This occurs when the magma is heated to a high temperature, causing it to partially melt and form a mixture of melt and solid minerals. As the magma cools, the minerals in the mixture can continue to grow and crystallize, resulting in a coarse-grained texture.

In addition to their formation processes, coarse-grained textures also provide valuable information about the conditions under which the magma was formed. For example, the size and shape of the grains can indicate the temperature and pressure conditions, as well as the rate of cooling. This information can be used to reconstruct the geological history of the Earth's crust and understand the processes that have shaped it over time.

In conclusion, coarse-grained textures are a defining characteristic of plutonic rocks and play a crucial role in understanding the formation and evolution of these rocks. By studying the textures of plutonic rocks, we can gain valuable insights into the processes and conditions that occur deep within the Earth's crust. 


# Advanced Igneous Petrology Textbook

## Chapter 4: Plutonic Rocks

 4.1: Plutonic Textures

Plutonic rocks are a type of igneous rock that is formed deep within the Earth's crust. They are characterized by their coarse-grained texture and their formation through the crystallization of molten rock, or magma. In this section, we will explore the different textures of plutonic rocks and how they are formed.

#### Subsection 4.1a: Coarse-grained Textures in Plutonic Rocks

Coarse-grained textures are a common feature of plutonic rocks. These textures are formed when the magma cools slowly, allowing the minerals to grow and crystallize in a larger size. This results in a coarse-grained texture, with visible grains that are typically larger than 2 millimeters in size.

The formation of coarse-grained textures is influenced by several factors, including the composition of the magma, the temperature and pressure conditions, and the rate of cooling. For example, magmas with a high silica content tend to produce coarse-grained textures, as the silica molecules hinder the growth of minerals and result in larger grains.

Coarse-grained textures are also commonly found in plutonic rocks that have undergone partial melting. This occurs when the magma is heated to a high temperature, causing it to partially melt and form a mixture of melt and solid minerals. As the magma cools, the minerals in the mixture can continue to grow and crystallize, resulting in a coarse-grained texture.

In addition to their formation processes, coarse-grained textures also provide valuable information about the conditions under which the magma was formed. For example, the size and shape of the grains can indicate the temperature and pressure conditions, as well as the rate of cooling. This information can be used to reconstruct the geological history of the Earth's crust and understand the processes that have shaped it over time.

### Subsection 4.1b: Fine-grained Textures in Plutonic Rocks

In contrast to coarse-grained textures, fine-grained textures are characterized by smaller grains, typically less than 2 millimeters in size. These textures are formed when the magma cools rapidly, preventing the minerals from growing and crystallizing in a larger size. This results in a fine-grained texture, with smaller grains that are difficult to see with the naked eye.

The formation of fine-grained textures is also influenced by the composition of the magma, as well as the temperature and pressure conditions. Magmas with a lower silica content tend to produce fine-grained textures, as the minerals can grow and crystallize more quickly in a lower silica environment.

Fine-grained textures are commonly found in plutonic rocks that have undergone rapid cooling, such as in volcanic arcs or in the presence of large amounts of water. These textures can also provide valuable information about the conditions under which the magma was formed, as well as the processes that have shaped the Earth's crust over time.

### Subsection 4.1c: Microtextures in Plutonic Rocks

In addition to coarse-grained and fine-grained textures, plutonic rocks can also exhibit microtextures. These textures are characterized by extremely small grains, typically less than 0.1 millimeters in size. They are formed when the magma cools very rapidly, preventing the minerals from growing and crystallizing in a larger size.

Microtextures are commonly found in plutonic rocks that have undergone extremely rapid cooling, such as in the presence of large amounts of water or in the formation of small intrusive igneous bodies. These textures can provide valuable information about the conditions under which the magma was formed, as well as the processes that have shaped the Earth's crust over time.

In conclusion, the texture of plutonic rocks is an important aspect of understanding their formation and evolution. Coarse-grained, fine-grained, and microtextures all provide valuable information about the conditions under which the magma was formed and the processes that have shaped the Earth's crust over time. By studying these textures, we can gain a deeper understanding of the complex and dynamic nature of plutonic rocks.


# Advanced Igneous Petrology Textbook

## Chapter 4: Plutonic Rocks

 4.2: Plutonic Mineralogy

Plutonic rocks are a type of igneous rock that is formed deep within the Earth's crust. They are characterized by their coarse-grained texture and their formation through the crystallization of molten rock, or magma. In this section, we will explore the different mineral compositions of plutonic rocks and how they are formed.

#### Subsection 4.2a: Common Minerals in Plutonic Rocks

Plutonic rocks are composed of a variety of minerals, each with its own unique properties and characteristics. Some of the most common minerals found in plutonic rocks include quartz, feldspar, mica, and amphibole.

Quartz is a common mineral found in many types of igneous rocks, including plutonic rocks. It is a silicate mineral with a hardness of 7 on the Mohs scale, making it resistant to weathering and erosion. Quartz is typically colorless or white, and its crystals are often intergrown with other minerals, giving it a granular or microcrystalline texture.

Feldspar is another common mineral found in plutonic rocks. It is a group of minerals that make up about 60% of the Earth's crust. Feldspar is typically light in color, with a hardness of 6 on the Mohs scale. It is commonly found in granitic rocks and is known for its ability to form large, euhedral crystals.

Mica is a group of minerals that are commonly found in plutonic rocks. They are characterized by their thin, platy shape and their ability to split into thin sheets. Mica is typically found in metamorphic rocks, but it can also be found in some igneous rocks, such as gneiss.

Amphibole is a group of minerals that are commonly found in plutonic rocks. They are characterized by their dark color and their prismatic crystals. Amphibole is typically found in mafic igneous rocks, such as basalt and gabbro.

The mineral composition of plutonic rocks is influenced by several factors, including the composition of the magma, the temperature and pressure conditions, and the rate of cooling. For example, magmas with a high silica content tend to produce more quartz and feldspar, while magmas with a lower silica content tend to produce more mica and amphibole.

In addition to their mineral composition, plutonic rocks also exhibit different textures, as discussed in the previous section. These textures can provide valuable information about the conditions under which the magma was formed and the processes that have shaped the Earth's crust over time.

### Subsection 4.2b: Mineral Compositions and Textures in Plutonic Rocks

The mineral composition and texture of plutonic rocks can vary greatly depending on the type of magma from which they were formed. For example, plutonic rocks formed from a silica-rich magma will have a different mineral composition and texture than those formed from a silica-poor magma.

In general, plutonic rocks formed from a silica-rich magma will have a more felsic texture, with a higher proportion of quartz and feldspar. These rocks are typically light in color and have a coarse-grained texture. On the other hand, plutonic rocks formed from a silica-poor magma will have a more mafic texture, with a higher proportion of mica and amphibole. These rocks are typically dark in color and have a fine-grained texture.

The mineral composition and texture of plutonic rocks can also provide valuable information about the processes that have shaped the Earth's crust. For example, the presence of certain minerals, such as biotite or hornblende, can indicate the presence of a magmatic water system. This can provide insights into the role of water in the formation of plutonic rocks and the overall geological history of the Earth's crust.

In conclusion, the mineral composition and texture of plutonic rocks are important aspects of understanding their formation and evolution. By studying the different minerals and textures found in these rocks, we can gain a deeper understanding of the processes that have shaped the Earth's crust over time. 


# Advanced Igneous Petrology Textbook

## Chapter 4: Plutonic Rocks




### Subsection 3.3b The role of granites in igneous rock series

Granites play a crucial role in the formation of igneous rock series. They are one of the most common types of felsic magmas and are responsible for the formation of many of the world's most well-known igneous rocks. In this section, we will explore the role of granites in igneous rock series and their significance in the study of igneous petrology.

#### The Formation of Granites

Granites are formed through the crystallization of felsic magmas. These magmas are typically derived from the melting of the Earth's mantle or the partial melting of the crust. The composition of the granite will depend on the source of the magma and the conditions it experiences during its formation and evolution.

One of the key factors that influence the formation of granites is the presence of water. As mentioned in the previous section, water can significantly increase the amount of melt produced during hydrous mantle melting. In granites, water can also play a crucial role in the formation of different minerals. For example, the presence of water can promote the formation of minerals such as plagioclase feldspar, which is a common mineral in granites.

#### The Significance of Granites in Igneous Rock Series

Granites are significant in the study of igneous petrology for several reasons. Firstly, they are one of the most common types of igneous rocks, making up a large portion of the Earth's crust. This means that studying granites can provide valuable insights into the formation and evolution of the Earth's crust.

Secondly, granites are often used as a reference point for understanding other types of igneous rocks. Their composition and mineralogy are well-studied, making them a useful comparison for understanding the formation and evolution of other types of igneous rocks.

Finally, granites are often associated with important geological events, such as plate boundaries and mountain building. Studying the formation and evolution of granites can provide insights into these events and their impact on the Earth's crust.

In conclusion, granites play a crucial role in the formation of igneous rock series and are a fundamental aspect of the study of igneous petrology. Their composition, mineralogy, and association with important geological events make them a valuable subject of study for understanding the Earth's crust and its evolution. 





### Subsection 3.4a Characteristics and formation of large silicic magmatic systems

Large silicic magmatic systems are a significant component of the Earth's crust, and their study is crucial for understanding the formation and evolution of igneous rocks. These systems are characterized by their high silica content, typically greater than 63%, and their association with important geological events such as plate boundaries and mountain building.

#### The Formation of Large Silicic Magmatic Systems

Large silicic magmatic systems are typically formed through the crystallization of felsic magmas. These magmas are derived from the melting of the Earth's mantle or the partial melting of the crust. The composition of the magma will depend on the source of the magma and the conditions it experiences during its formation and evolution.

One of the key factors that influence the formation of large silicic magmatic systems is the presence of water. As mentioned in the previous section, water can significantly increase the amount of melt produced during hydrous mantle melting. In large silicic magmatic systems, water can also play a crucial role in the formation of different minerals. For example, the presence of water can promote the formation of minerals such as plagioclase feldspar, which is a common mineral in these systems.

#### The Significance of Large Silicic Magmatic Systems in Igneous Rock Series

Large silicic magmatic systems are significant in the study of igneous petrology for several reasons. Firstly, they are one of the most common types of igneous rocks, making up a large portion of the Earth's crust. This means that studying large silicic magmatic systems can provide valuable insights into the formation and evolution of the Earth's crust.

Secondly, large silicic magmatic systems are often used as a reference point for understanding other types of igneous rocks. Their composition and mineralogy are well-studied, making them a useful comparison for understanding the formation and evolution of other types of igneous rocks.

Finally, large silicic magmatic systems are often associated with important geological events, such as plate boundaries and mountain building. Studying the formation and evolution of these systems can provide insights into these events and their impact on the Earth's crust.
