## Chapter: - Chapter 5: Igneous Petrogenesis:

### Introduction

Welcome to Chapter 5 of the "Advanced Igneous Petrology Textbook". In this chapter, we will delve into the fascinating world of igneous petrogenesis. This is the process by which igneous rocks are formed, and it is a crucial aspect of understanding the Earth's crust and mantle.

Igneous petrology is a branch of petrology that deals with the study of igneous rocks. These rocks are formed from the solidification of molten rock, or magma, and they provide valuable insights into the Earth's interior processes. The study of igneous petrology is essential for understanding the formation of the Earth's crust and the evolution of the planet.

In this chapter, we will explore the various factors that influence igneous petrogenesis, including the composition of the magma, the conditions under which it solidifies, and the interaction with the surrounding environment. We will also discuss the different types of igneous rocks and their characteristics, as well as the methods used to study them.

The study of igneous petrology is a complex and multidisciplinary field, and it is constantly evolving as new techniques and technologies are developed. This chapter aims to provide a comprehensive overview of the subject, covering the fundamental principles and concepts, as well as the latest advancements in the field.

Whether you are a student, a researcher, or simply a curious reader, we hope that this chapter will enhance your understanding of igneous petrology and its importance in the study of the Earth. So, let's embark on this exciting journey together and explore the fascinating world of igneous petrogenesis.




### Subsection: 5.1a Partial melting in the mantle

Partial melting is a fundamental process in the formation of igneous rocks. It occurs when the temperature and pressure conditions in the mantle cause the melting of only a portion of the mantle material. This process is crucial in the formation of different types of igneous rocks, as it allows for the mixing of different mantle materials and the release of volatile elements.

#### 5.1a.1 Mechanisms of Partial Melting

There are several mechanisms by which partial melting can occur in the mantle. These include adiabatic heating, conduction, and convection. Adiabatic heating occurs when a mass of mantle material rises due to buoyancy, and as it rises, it cools adiabatically, leading to the melting of a portion of the material. Conduction and convection, on the other hand, occur due to the transfer of heat from the core-mantle boundary to the mantle. Conduction is the transfer of heat through direct contact, while convection involves the movement of mantle material due to differences in temperature and density.

#### 5.1a.2 Factors Influencing Partial Melting

The amount and composition of the melt produced during partial melting are influenced by several factors. These include the composition of the mantle material, the temperature and pressure conditions, and the presence of volatiles. The composition of the mantle material plays a crucial role in determining the amount of melt produced. For instance, the presence of olivine in the mantle can lead to the production of a large amount of melt, while the presence of pyroxene can result in a smaller amount of melt.

The temperature and pressure conditions in the mantle also play a significant role in partial melting. As the temperature and pressure increase, the melting point of the mantle material decreases, leading to the production of more melt. The presence of volatiles, such as water, can also lower the melting point of the mantle material, promoting partial melting.

#### 5.1a.3 Implications of Partial Melting

Partial melting has significant implications for the formation of igneous rocks. It allows for the mixing of different mantle materials, leading to the production of heterogeneous melts. This mixing can result in the formation of different types of igneous rocks, each with its own unique composition and characteristics. Partial melting also plays a crucial role in the release of volatile elements, which can have a significant impact on the Earth's atmosphere and climate.

In conclusion, partial melting is a complex process that plays a crucial role in the formation of igneous rocks. It is influenced by various factors and has significant implications for the Earth's crust and mantle. In the next section, we will explore the different types of igneous rocks and their characteristics, as well as the methods used to study them.


# Advanced Igneous Petrology Textbook":

## Chapter 5: Igneous Petrogenesis:




### Subsection: 5.1b Mantle plumes and hotspots

Mantle plumes and hotspots are two important concepts in the study of igneous petrology. They are responsible for the formation of many of the world's volcanoes and oceanic islands, and understanding their behavior is crucial for understanding the dynamics of the Earth's mantle.

#### 5.1b.1 Mantle Plumes

Mantle plumes are columns of hot, buoyant material that rise from the core-mantle boundary to the surface. They are typically associated with hotspots, but not all hotspots are necessarily associated with mantle plumes. The concept of mantle plumes was first proposed by Xavier Le Pichon in the 1970s, who suggested that the Hawaiian Islands were formed by a hotspot that had been active for millions of years.

The behavior of mantle plumes is still not fully understood, and there are several competing theories about their formation and behavior. One theory suggests that mantle plumes are formed by the melting of a thin layer of low-density material at the base of the mantle, which then rises due to buoyancy. Another theory suggests that mantle plumes are formed by the melting of a thick layer of high-density material at the base of the mantle, which then rises due to adiabatic heating as it cools and expands.

#### 5.1b.2 Hotspots

Hotspots are regions of the Earth's surface where volcanic activity is significantly higher than the surrounding areas. They are typically associated with mantle plumes, but not all hotspots are necessarily associated with mantle plumes. The Hawaiian Islands, the Yellowstone Caldera, and the Iceland hotspot are all examples of hotspots.

The behavior of hotspots is also not fully understood. Some hotspots, such as the Hawaiian Islands, have been active for millions of years, while others, such as the Yellowstone Caldera, have been active for only a few hundred thousand years. The reason for this variability is still not fully understood.

#### 5.1b.3 Chemistry, Heat Flow, and Melting

The chemistry and heat flow of mantle plumes and hotspots are crucial for understanding their behavior. The chemical and isotopic composition of basalts found at hotspots differs subtly from mid-ocean ridge basalts. These basalts, also called ocean island basalts (OIBs), are analyzed in their radiogenic and stable isotope compositions. In radiogenic isotope systems, the originally subducted material creates diverging trends, termed mantle components. Identified mantle components are DMM (depleted mid-ocean ridge basalt (MORB) mantle), HIMU (high U/Pb-ratio mantle), EM1 (enriched mantle 1), EM2 (enriched mantle 2) and FOZO (focus zone). This geochemical signature arises from the mixing of near-surface materials such as subducted slabs and continental sediments, in the mantle source.

Stable isotopes like Fe are used to trace the origin of these mantle components. For example, the presence of high FeO content in OIBs is indicative of a deep mantle origin, while low FeO content is indicative of a shallow mantle origin. The heat flow at hotspots is also important, as it can provide insights into the behavior of mantle plumes. The heat flow at hotspots is typically higher than at other regions of the Earth's surface, reflecting the presence of mantle plumes.

In conclusion, mantle plumes and hotspots are two important concepts in the study of igneous petrology. Their behavior and the chemistry and heat flow associated with them are still not fully understood, but ongoing research is shedding light on these complex phenomena.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the fascinating world of igneous petrogenesis, exploring the processes that lead to the formation of igneous rocks. We have learned that igneous rocks are formed through the crystallization of molten rock, or magma, which is derived from the Earth's mantle. The composition of the magma, and hence the resulting igneous rock, is influenced by a variety of factors, including the composition of the mantle, the presence of volatiles, and the pressure and temperature conditions under which the magma forms.

We have also examined the different types of igneous rocks, including extrusive and intrusive rocks, and how they are formed. Extrusive rocks, such as basalt and andesite, are formed at the surface of the Earth, while intrusive rocks, such as granite and gneiss, are formed deep within the Earth's crust. The formation of these rocks involves complex processes, including fractional melting, differentiation, and crystallization.

Finally, we have discussed the role of igneous petrology in understanding the evolution of the Earth's crust and mantle. By studying the composition and structure of igneous rocks, we can gain insights into the processes that have shaped our planet over billions of years. This knowledge is crucial for a wide range of fields, including geology, geophysics, and environmental science.

## Chapter: Chapter 6: The Role of Volatiles in Igneous Petrology

### Introduction

The study of igneous petrology is a fascinating journey into the heart of the Earth, exploring the processes that shape our planet's crust and mantle. In this chapter, we delve deeper into the role of volatiles in igneous petrology, a critical aspect that has been largely overlooked in traditional petrology textbooks. 

Volatiles, substances with low boiling points, play a pivotal role in the formation and evolution of igneous rocks. They are the key to understanding the complex interplay between the Earth's mantle and crust, and their influence extends to the formation of ore deposits and the evolution of life. 

This chapter will provide a comprehensive overview of the role of volatiles in igneous petrology, exploring their sources, behavior, and impact on the formation of different types of igneous rocks. We will also delve into the latest research and advancements in this field, shedding light on the intricate processes that govern the Earth's interior.

From the formation of basaltic magmas to the genesis of granitic rocks, the role of volatiles is a central theme in igneous petrology. This chapter aims to equip readers with a solid understanding of these processes, providing the necessary tools to interpret the complex chemical and mineralogical signatures of igneous rocks.

As we navigate through this chapter, we will also touch upon the environmental implications of volatiles in igneous petrology. The release of volatiles during volcanic eruptions, for instance, can have profound effects on the atmosphere and climate. Understanding these processes is not only crucial for petrologists but also for environmental scientists and policymakers.

In essence, this chapter aims to provide a comprehensive and accessible introduction to the role of volatiles in igneous petrology. It is our hope that this chapter will serve as a valuable resource for students, researchers, and anyone interested in the fascinating world of igneous petrology.




### Subsection: 5.2a Fractional crystallization

Fractional crystallization is a fundamental process in the differentiation of magmas. It is a process by which different minerals crystallize from a melt at different temperatures, leading to the formation of different types of igneous rocks. This process is crucial in understanding the evolution of magmas and the formation of different types of volcanic rocks.

#### 5.2a.1 Basic Principles of Fractional Crystallization

Fractional crystallization is based on the principle that different minerals have different melting points. As a magma cools, minerals with higher melting points will crystallize first, leaving behind a residual melt with a different chemical composition. This residual melt can then continue to cool and crystallize, leading to the formation of different types of igneous rocks.

The efficiency of fractional crystallization is determined by the crystallization behavior of the minerals present in the magma. Minerals with high crystallization efficiencies, such as olivine and pyroxene, will crystallize early and remove a large amount of melt from the system. In contrast, minerals with low crystallization efficiencies, such as plagioclase feldspar, will crystallize later and remove a smaller amount of melt.

#### 5.2a.2 Types of Fractional Crystallization

There are two main types of fractional crystallization: batch and continuous. In batch fractional crystallization, the magma is cooled and crystallized in a single batch, with the crystals being removed from the system. This type of fractional crystallization is common in intrusive igneous processes, where the magma is trapped in a chamber and cools slowly.

In continuous fractional crystallization, the magma is continuously cooled and crystallized, with the crystals being removed from the system as the magma continues to cool. This type of fractional crystallization is common in extrusive igneous processes, where the magma is erupted at the surface and cools rapidly.

#### 5.2a.3 Factors Affecting Fractional Crystallization

The efficiency of fractional crystallization is influenced by several factors, including the composition of the magma, the temperature and pressure conditions, and the crystallization behavior of the minerals present. Magmas with higher silica contents tend to have lower crystallization efficiencies, as the minerals with higher melting points, such as olivine and pyroxene, are more likely to crystallize early and remove a large amount of melt from the system.

Temperature and pressure conditions also play a crucial role in fractional crystallization. Higher temperatures and pressures can delay the crystallization of minerals, allowing for more efficient fractional crystallization to occur. Conversely, lower temperatures and pressures can lead to earlier crystallization of minerals, reducing the efficiency of fractional crystallization.

The crystallization behavior of the minerals present in the magma is also a significant factor in fractional crystallization. Minerals with high crystallization efficiencies, such as olivine and pyroxene, will crystallize early and remove a large amount of melt from the system. In contrast, minerals with low crystallization efficiencies, such as plagioclase feldspar, will crystallize later and remove a smaller amount of melt.

#### 5.2a.4 Applications of Fractional Crystallization

Fractional crystallization has many applications in the study of igneous petrology. It is used to understand the evolution of magmas and the formation of different types of volcanic rocks. By studying the mineral assemblages and chemical compositions of different types of igneous rocks, geologists can reconstruct the processes by which they were formed, including the role of fractional crystallization.

Fractional crystallization is also used in the extraction of valuable minerals from magmatic systems. By controlling the conditions of temperature and pressure, as well as the composition of the magma, it is possible to optimize the crystallization of specific minerals, allowing for their efficient extraction.

In conclusion, fractional crystallization is a crucial process in the differentiation of magmas and the formation of different types of igneous rocks. By understanding the basic principles and factors influencing this process, geologists can gain valuable insights into the evolution of magmas and the formation of volcanic rocks. 





### Subsection: 5.2b Magma mixing

Magma mixing is a crucial process in the differentiation of magmas. It occurs when two or more magmas meet, comele, and form a magma of a composition somewhere between the two end-member magmas. This process is common in volcanic magma chambers, which are open-system chambers where magmas enter the chamber, undergo some form of assimilation, fractional crystallisation and partial melt extraction (via eruption of lava), and are replenished.

#### 5.2b.1 Mechanisms of Magma Mixing

There are two main mechanisms of magma mixing: conduit mixing and chamber mixing. Conduit mixing occurs when magmas are mixed as they move through a conduit towards the surface. This can happen due to the mixing of different magmas entering the conduit from different sources, or due to the mixing of a single magma as it moves through the conduit.

Chamber mixing, on the other hand, occurs within the magma chamber itself. This can happen due to the mixing of different magmas entering the chamber from different sources, or due to the mixing of a single magma as it moves through the chamber. Chamber mixing can also occur due to the movement of magmas within the chamber, such as during the Kīlauea Iki eruption where two different magma sources provided material to the surface.

#### 5.2b.2 Implications of Magma Mixing

Magma mixing has significant implications for the composition and evolution of magmas. By mixing, magmas can exchange heat and mass, leading to changes in their chemical and mineralogical composition. This can result in the formation of new minerals, the alteration of existing minerals, and the creation of new types of igneous rocks.

In the case of the Kīlauea Iki eruption, magma mixing led to the formation of a new type of magma with a high ratio of CaO to MgO. This magma was a mixture of the two different magma sources, and its composition was somewhere between the two end-member magmas. The subsequent eruption episodes at the single vent on the west side of the caldera contained a mixture of the two magmas, plus some additional amount of olivine. This suggests that the magma chamber was not homogeneous, and that different magma sources were contributing to the eruption.

#### 5.2b.3 Magma Mixing and Igneous Petrogenesis

Magma mixing plays a crucial role in the petrogenesis of igneous rocks. By mixing, magmas can exchange heat and mass, leading to changes in their chemical and mineralogical composition. This can result in the formation of new minerals, the alteration of existing minerals, and the creation of new types of igneous rocks. Understanding the mechanisms and implications of magma mixing is therefore essential for understanding the petrogenesis of igneous rocks.




### Subsection: 5.3a Magma ascent mechanisms

The ascent of magma is a complex process that involves a series of physical and chemical interactions. The mechanisms of magma ascent are crucial to understanding the formation of igneous rocks and the evolution of magmas. In this section, we will explore the two main mechanisms of magma ascent: Stokes diapirism and fracture propagation.

#### 5.3a.1 Stokes Diapirism

Stokes diapirism is a mechanism of magma ascent that involves the buoyancy of the magma. The magma, being less dense than the surrounding rocks, rises through the crust as a single mass. As it rises, it heats the wall rocks, causing them to behave as a power-law fluid and thus flow around the intrusion allowing it to pass without major heat loss. This mechanism is feasible in the warm, ductile lower crust where rocks are easily deformed, but runs into problems in the upper crust which is far colder and more brittle. Rocks there do not deform so easily: for magma to rise as a diapir it would expend far too much energy in heating wall rocks, thus cooling and solidifying before reaching higher levels within the crust.

#### 5.3a.2 Fracture Propagation

Fracture propagation is another mechanism of magma ascent that involves the propagation of fractures along which the magma can move. These fractures form along self-propagating dykes which form along new or pre-existing fracture or fault systems and networks of active shear zones. As these narrow conduits open, the first magma to enter solidifies and provides a form of insulation for later magma. This mechanism is particularly effective in the upper crust, where the rocks are colder and more brittle.

These mechanisms can operate in tandem. For example, diapirs may continue to rise through the brittle upper crust through stoping, where the granite cracks the roof rocks, removing blocks of the overlying crust which then sink to the bottom of the diapir while the magma rises through the fractures.

In the next section, we will explore the implications of these magma ascent mechanisms for the formation of igneous rocks and the evolution of magmas.

### Subsection: 5.3b Emplacement mechanisms

The emplacement of magma is a critical step in the formation of igneous rocks. It involves the process by which magma is injected into the crust and forms intrusive igneous bodies. The mechanisms of magma emplacement are diverse and complex, and they are influenced by a variety of factors, including the composition of the magma, the properties of the surrounding rocks, and the tectonic setting.

#### 5.3b.1 Sill Emplacement

Sill emplacement is a common mechanism of magma emplacement. A sill is a tabular igneous body that is bounded by horizontal surfaces and is typically emplaced in the lower part of the crust. The emplacement of sills is often associated with the extrusion of lava flows. The magma that forms sills is typically basaltic in composition and is injected into the crust along fractures or faults. The emplacement of sills can occur through a variety of mechanisms, including diapiric rise, fracture propagation, and stoping.

#### 5.3b.2 Batholith Emplacement

Batholith emplacement is another important mechanism of magma emplacement. A batholith is a large, intrusive igneous body that is typically composed of granitic rocks. The emplacement of batholiths is often associated with the formation of mountain ranges and other orogenic features. The magma that forms batholiths is typically more evolved than the magma that forms sills, and it is often injected into the crust through a series of intrusive events.

#### 5.3b.3 Pluton Emplacement

Pluton emplacement is a mechanism of magma emplacement that involves the intrusion of magma into the crust through a series of intrusive events. A pluton is an igneous body that is bounded by vertical surfaces and is typically composed of granitic rocks. The emplacement of plutons is often associated with the formation of mountain ranges and other orogenic features. The magma that forms plutons is typically more evolved than the magma that forms batholiths, and it is often injected into the crust through a series of intrusive events.

#### 5.3b.4 Volcanic Emplacement

Volcanic emplacement is a mechanism of magma emplacement that involves the extrusion of magma onto the surface of the Earth. This can occur through a variety of processes, including lava domes, lava flows, and pyroclastic flows. The composition of the magma that is extruded can vary widely, from basaltic to rhyolitic, and the emplacement of volcanic rocks can occur through a variety of mechanisms, including diapiric rise, fracture propagation, and stoping.

In the next section, we will explore the implications of these emplacement mechanisms for the formation of igneous rocks and the evolution of magmas.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the complex and fascinating world of igneous petrogenesis, exploring the processes that lead to the formation of igneous rocks. We have examined the various factors that influence these processes, including the composition of the magma, the tectonic setting, and the interaction with the surrounding rocks. We have also discussed the different types of igneous rocks and their characteristics, providing a comprehensive understanding of the subject.

The chapter has also highlighted the importance of understanding the petrogenesis of igneous rocks in various fields, including geology, geophysics, and mineral exploration. The knowledge gained from this chapter will be invaluable in these fields, as it will enable us to better understand the formation of igneous rocks and their role in the Earth's crust.

In conclusion, the study of igneous petrogenesis is a vast and complex field, but with a solid understanding of the principles and processes involved, we can gain a deeper appreciation of the Earth's crust and its evolution.

## Chapter: Chapter 6: Igneous Textures and Microstructures

### Introduction

The study of igneous petrology is a fascinating journey into the heart of the Earth, exploring the processes that shape our planet's crust and mantle. In this chapter, we delve into the intricate world of igneous textures and microstructures, a critical aspect of understanding the formation and evolution of igneous rocks.

Igneous textures and microstructures are the physical manifestations of the processes that occur during the formation and cooling of magma. They provide a window into the conditions under which the magma was formed, the processes it underwent during its journey to the surface, and the interactions it had with the surrounding rocks. 

The study of these textures and microstructures is not just about understanding the past, but also about predicting the future. By understanding the processes that create these textures and microstructures, we can better predict how future volcanic eruptions might occur, how they might affect the environment, and how we might mitigate their impact.

In this chapter, we will explore the various types of igneous textures and microstructures, their formation processes, and their significance in the broader context of igneous petrology. We will also discuss the methods and techniques used to study these textures and microstructures, and how they contribute to our understanding of igneous rocks.

This chapter aims to provide a comprehensive overview of igneous textures and microstructures, equipping readers with the knowledge and tools to explore this fascinating field further. Whether you are a student, a researcher, or simply a curious reader, we hope that this chapter will ignite your interest in the world of igneous petrology.




### Subsection: 5.3b Magma emplacement processes

The emplacement of magma is a critical step in the formation of igneous rocks. It involves the process of magma moving from its source to the surface, where it is eventually erupted. The emplacement of magma is influenced by a variety of factors, including the composition of the magma, the structure of the crust, and the presence of fluids.

#### 5.3b.1 Magma Chamber Formation

Magma chambers are the primary sites of magma emplacement. These chambers can be located at various depths beneath the surface, and their size and shape can vary significantly. The formation of a magma chamber involves the accumulation of magma at a specific location. This can occur through the ascent of magma from the mantle, the mixing of different magmas, or the accumulation of magma from hydrothermal systems.

The composition of the magma can also play a significant role in the formation of a magma chamber. For instance, the presence of volatile elements such as water, carbon dioxide, and sulfur can lower the melting point of the magma, promoting its accumulation in a chamber. Additionally, the composition of the magma can influence its viscosity, which can affect its ability to flow and fill a chamber.

#### 5.3b.2 Magma Emplacement Mechanisms

The emplacement of magma can occur through various mechanisms, including diapiric intrusion, sill formation, and dyke propagation. Diapiric intrusion, as discussed in the previous section, involves the buoyancy of the magma as it rises through the crust. Sill formation, on the other hand, involves the horizontal movement of magma beneath the crust, forming a sheet-like intrusion. Dyke propagation involves the vertical movement of magma along fractures or faults, forming narrow, elongated intrusions.

The choice of emplacement mechanism can be influenced by the composition of the magma, the structure of the crust, and the presence of fluids. For instance, diapiric intrusion is more likely to occur in warm, ductile lower crust, while sill formation is more likely to occur in cooler, more brittle upper crust. Dyke propagation, on the other hand, can occur in both the lower and upper crust, and is often influenced by the presence of pre-existing fractures or faults.

#### 5.3b.3 Magma-Hydrothermal Interactions

Interactions between magma and hydrothermal systems can also play a significant role in magma emplacement. Hydrothermal systems, which involve the circulation of hot fluids through the crust, can interact with magma in various ways. For instance, hydrothermal fluids can introduce volatile elements into the magma, lowering its melting point and promoting its accumulation in a chamber. Additionally, hydrothermal fluids can alter the composition of the magma, leading to the formation of new minerals and the precipitation of others.

In some cases, hydrothermal systems can even drive the emplacement of magma. For instance, the circulation of hydrothermal fluids can create pressure gradients that drive the movement of magma. This can occur in systems where the magma is not buoyant enough to rise on its own, but where the circulation of hydrothermal fluids creates a pressure gradient that drives the magma upward.

In conclusion, the emplacement of magma is a complex process that is influenced by a variety of factors. Understanding these processes is crucial for understanding the formation of igneous rocks and the evolution of magmas.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the fascinating world of igneous petrogenesis, exploring the processes that lead to the formation of igneous rocks. We have learned that igneous rocks are formed through the crystallization of molten rock, or magma, which is derived from the mantle, crust, or other sources. The composition and characteristics of the resulting igneous rock depend on a variety of factors, including the source of the magma, the conditions under which it crystallizes, and the presence of other elements or compounds.

We have also examined the different types of igneous rocks, including plutonic and volcanic rocks, and how they are formed. Plutonic rocks, which are formed deep within the Earth's crust, are typically coarse-grained and have undergone significant crystallization. Volcanic rocks, on the other hand, are formed at the surface of the Earth and are often fine-grained and glassy.

Finally, we have discussed the role of igneous petrology in understanding the evolution of the Earth's crust and mantle. By studying the composition and characteristics of igneous rocks, we can gain insights into the processes that have shaped our planet over billions of years.

## Chapter: Chapter 6: Igneous Textures and Mineral Assemblages

### Introduction

The study of igneous petrology is a fascinating journey into the heart of the Earth, exploring the processes that shape our planet's crust and mantle. In this chapter, we delve into the intricate world of igneous textures and mineral assemblages, two fundamental aspects of igneous petrology.

Igneous textures refer to the arrangement of minerals within an igneous rock. They provide valuable insights into the conditions under which the rock was formed, including temperature, pressure, and the rate of cooling. Understanding these textures can help us decipher the history of the rock, from its origin as molten magma to its solidification and cooling.

Mineral assemblages, on the other hand, are the collection of minerals found in an igneous rock. Each mineral has its own unique properties and characteristics, which can reveal important information about the rock's composition and formation. By studying these assemblages, we can gain a deeper understanding of the processes that led to the formation of the rock.

In this chapter, we will explore the various types of igneous textures and mineral assemblages, and how they are formed. We will also discuss the techniques and tools used to study these aspects of igneous petrology, including microscopy and spectroscopy. By the end of this chapter, you will have a solid foundation in these topics, equipping you with the knowledge and skills to further explore the fascinating world of igneous petrology.




### Subsection: 5.4a Eruption styles and products

Volcanism is a complex process that involves the eruption of magma from the mantle to the surface. The style of an eruption, or the way in which magma is expelled from a volcano, is determined by the composition of the magma, the structure of the volcanic system, and the presence of fluids. The products of an eruption, or the materials that are expelled during an eruption, can provide valuable insights into the processes that occur during volcanism.

#### 5.4a.1 Eruption Styles

Eruptions can be classified into two main styles: explosive and effusive. Explosive eruptions are characterized by the violent expulsion of magma, often accompanied by the formation of volcanic bombs and ash clouds. These eruptions are typically associated with high-viscosity magmas, which are resistant to flow and tend to fragment under the high pressures and temperatures of the volcanic conduit.

Effusive eruptions, on the other hand, involve the smooth and continuous extrusion of magma. These eruptions are typically associated with low-viscosity magmas, which are more fluid and tend to flow rather than fragment under the lower pressures and temperatures of the volcanic conduit. Effusive eruptions can produce a variety of products, including lava flows, spatter cones, and pahoehoe.

#### 5.4a.2 Products of Eruptions

The products of an eruption can provide valuable insights into the processes that occur during volcanism. These products can include lava flows, spatter cones, pahoehoe, and ash clouds.

Lava flows are the most common product of volcanic eruptions. They are formed when magma is extruded from a volcanic conduit and flows down the volcanic edifice. The composition of the lava flow can provide insights into the composition of the magma and the processes that occur during volcanism.

Spatter cones are another common product of volcanic eruptions. They are formed when magma is ejected from a volcanic conduit and forms a cone of solidified magma. The composition and structure of the spatter cone can provide insights into the composition of the magma and the processes that occur during volcanism.

Pahoehoe is a type of lava flow that is characterized by its smooth, ropy surface. It is formed when low-viscosity magmas are extruded from a volcanic conduit and flow down the volcanic edifice. The composition and structure of the pahoehoe can provide insights into the composition of the magma and the processes that occur during volcanism.

Ash clouds are a common product of explosive eruptions. They are formed when magma is violently expelled from a volcanic conduit and breaks up into small fragments. These fragments can range in size from fine ash to large volcanic bombs. The composition and structure of the ash cloud can provide insights into the composition of the magma and the processes that occur during volcanism.

In conclusion, the study of eruption styles and products is a crucial aspect of volcanology. It allows us to understand the processes that occur during volcanism and provides valuable insights into the composition of magmas and the structure of volcanic systems.




### Subsection: 5.4b Volcano monitoring and hazard assessment

Volcano monitoring and hazard assessment are crucial aspects of volcanology. They involve the use of various techniques to monitor volcanic activity and assess the potential hazards associated with volcanic eruptions. This section will discuss the techniques used in volcano monitoring and hazard assessment.

#### 5.4b.1 Techniques for Volcano Monitoring

Volcano monitoring involves the use of various techniques to monitor volcanic activity. These techniques can be broadly categorized into two types: remote sensing techniques and direct monitoring techniques.

Remote sensing techniques involve the use of instruments that can detect and measure volcanic activity from a distance. These techniques include seismic monitoring, satellite imaging, and gas monitoring.

Seismic monitoring involves the use of seismometers to detect and record seismic activity. Seismic activity, such as earthquakes and volcanic tremors, can provide valuable information about the state of a volcanic system. For example, an increase in seismic activity can indicate an impending eruption.

Satellite imaging involves the use of satellites to capture images of volcanic systems. These images can provide valuable information about the state of a volcanic system, including changes in the size and shape of volcanic structures, the presence of volcanic clouds, and the movement of lava flows.

Gas monitoring involves the use of instruments to measure the composition and quantity of gases emitted by a volcano. These gases can provide valuable information about the composition of the magma and the processes occurring within the volcanic system.

Direct monitoring techniques involve the use of instruments that are placed directly on or near a volcano to monitor its activity. These techniques include ground deformation monitoring, temperature monitoring, and direct gas sampling.

Ground deformation monitoring involves the use of instruments to measure changes in the size and shape of a volcanic structure. These changes can provide valuable information about the movement of magma within the volcanic system and the potential for an eruption.

Temperature monitoring involves the use of instruments to measure the temperature of a volcanic system. Changes in temperature can provide valuable information about the state of a volcanic system, including the movement of magma and the potential for an eruption.

Direct gas sampling involves the collection of gases directly from a volcanic system for analysis. This technique can provide valuable information about the composition of the magma and the processes occurring within the volcanic system.

#### 5.4b.2 Hazard Assessment

Hazard assessment involves the use of various techniques to assess the potential hazards associated with volcanic eruptions. These techniques can be broadly categorized into two types: qualitative hazard assessment and quantitative hazard assessment.

Qualitative hazard assessment involves the use of expert judgment to assess the potential hazards associated with volcanic eruptions. This can be based on the interpretation of volcanic activity, the characteristics of the volcanic system, and historical eruptions.

Quantitative hazard assessment involves the use of mathematical models to estimate the potential hazards associated with volcanic eruptions. These models can be used to estimate the size and intensity of eruptions, the distribution of volcanic products, and the impact of these products on the environment and society.

In conclusion, volcano monitoring and hazard assessment are crucial aspects of volcanology. They involve the use of various techniques to monitor volcanic activity and assess the potential hazards associated with volcanic eruptions. These techniques are essential for understanding volcanic systems and mitigating the impacts of volcanic eruptions.

We have learned that igneous petrogenesis is a complex process that involves the melting of the Earth's mantle and crust, the separation of minerals, and the cooling and solidification of the resulting magma. 

We have also discussed the various factors that influence igneous petrogenesis, including the composition of the parent material, the pressure and temperature conditions, and the presence of fluids. We have seen how these factors can lead to the formation of different types of igneous rocks, each with its own unique characteristics.

Finally, we have examined the role of igneous petrogenesis in the evolution of the Earth's crust and the formation of new landforms. We have learned that igneous activity plays a crucial role in shaping the Earth's surface, and that understanding igneous petrogenesis is key to understanding the history and future of our planet.

## Chapter: - Chapter 6: Igneous Rock Associations:

### Introduction

In the previous chapters, we have explored the fundamental concepts of igneous petrology, including the formation and characteristics of different types of igneous rocks. In this chapter, we will delve deeper into the study of igneous rocks by examining their associations. 

Igneous rock associations refer to the grouping of different types of igneous rocks that occur together in a particular geological setting. These associations can provide valuable insights into the processes that formed them and the conditions under which they were formed. 

In this chapter, we will explore the various factors that influence the formation of igneous rock associations, including the composition of the parental magma, the pressure and temperature conditions during crystallization, and the presence of other minerals or rocks. We will also discuss the different types of igneous rock associations, such as the mafic-to-felsic series and the calc-alkaline series, and their significance in understanding the evolution of igneous systems.

By the end of this chapter, you will have a comprehensive understanding of the complex interplay between different types of igneous rocks and the factors that control their formation. This knowledge will not only enhance your understanding of igneous petrology but also equip you with the tools to interpret the geological history of different regions. 

So, let's embark on this exciting journey to explore the fascinating world of igneous rock associations.




### Subsection: 6.1a Mid-ocean ridge basalts (MORBs)

Mid-ocean ridges are underwater mountain ranges that form the backbone of the oceanic crust. They are sites of intense volcanic activity and are responsible for the creation of new oceanic crust. The volcanism at mid-ocean ridges is primarily driven by the process of seafloor spreading, where new crust is created at the ridge axis and moves away from the axis as the older crust is subducted into the mantle.

The volcanic rocks formed at mid-ocean ridges are known as mid-ocean ridge basalts (MORBs). These rocks are primarily composed of plagioclase feldspar, pyroxene, and olivine, and are typically dark in color due to their high iron and magnesium content. The composition of MORBs is largely controlled by the melting of the upper mantle, which is enriched in iron and magnesium compared to the Earth's crust.

#### 6.1a.1 Composition of MORBs

The composition of MORBs is largely controlled by the melting of the upper mantle. The mantle is composed of peridotite, a rock rich in iron and magnesium. As the mantle rises towards the surface at mid-ocean ridges, it undergoes decompression melting, which results in the formation of basaltic magma. This magma then cools and solidifies to form MORBs.

The composition of MORBs can vary depending on the location and the specific processes occurring at the mid-ocean ridge. However, in general, MORBs are low in silica (SiO<sub>2</sub>) and are classified as tholeiitic basalts. This means that they are enriched in iron and magnesium, and have a high affinity for iron.

#### 6.1a.2 Formation of MORBs

The formation of MORBs is a complex process that involves the interaction of various factors. These include the composition of the mantle, the pressure and temperature conditions at the mid-ocean ridge, and the presence of other minerals or rocks.

The mantle is the primary source of the magma that forms MORBs. The mantle is composed of peridotite, a rock rich in iron and magnesium. As the mantle rises towards the surface at mid-ocean ridges, it undergoes decompression melting, which results in the formation of basaltic magma.

The pressure and temperature conditions at the mid-ocean ridge also play a crucial role in the formation of MORBs. As the mantle rises towards the surface, it experiences a decrease in pressure and an increase in temperature. This change in conditions can affect the melting behavior of the mantle and can result in the formation of different types of MORBs.

The presence of other minerals or rocks can also influence the formation of MORBs. For example, the presence of ultramafic rocks, such as dunite, can result in the formation of more iron-rich MORBs. Similarly, the presence of mafic-to-felsic series rocks can result in the formation of more silica-rich MORBs.

#### 6.1a.3 Significance of MORBs

MORBs play a crucial role in the study of igneous petrology. They provide valuable insights into the processes occurring at mid-ocean ridges and the composition of the mantle. By studying the composition and formation of MORBs, scientists can gain a better understanding of the dynamics of the Earth's mantle and the processes that drive plate tectonics.

Furthermore, MORBs are also important in the study of the Earth's early history. The presence of certain elements, such as iron and magnesium, in MORBs can provide clues about the composition of the Earth's early mantle. This information can be used to reconstruct the conditions that existed on Earth billions of years ago.

In conclusion, MORBs are a crucial component of the oceanic crust and play a significant role in the study of igneous petrology. Their composition and formation are influenced by a variety of factors, and their study can provide valuable insights into the processes occurring at mid-ocean ridges and the early history of the Earth. 





### Subsection: 6.1b Ocean island basalts (OIBs)

Ocean island basalts (OIBs) are a type of igneous rock found on oceanic islands. They are formed by the melting of the mantle, similar to MORBs, but the processes involved in their formation are distinct. OIBs are known for their diverse compositions and their role in understanding the Earth's mantle dynamics.

#### 6.1b.1 Composition of OIBs

The composition of OIBs is highly variable, making them a rich source of information about the Earth's mantle. Unlike MORBs, which are primarily tholeiitic basalts, OIBs can be either tholeiitic or alkali basalts. Tholeiitic OIBs are enriched in iron and magnesium, similar to MORBs, but alkali OIBs are enriched in sodium and potassium.

The composition of OIBs is also influenced by the presence of mantle plumes, which are columns of hot, buoyant mantle material that rise from the core-mantle boundary. These plumes can introduce additional chemical components into the mantle, leading to the formation of OIBs with unique compositions.

#### 6.1b.2 Formation of OIBs

The formation of OIBs is a complex process that involves the interaction of various factors, including mantle plumes, subduction, and the presence of hotspots.

Mantle plumes play a crucial role in the formation of OIBs. These plumes can introduce additional chemical components into the mantle, leading to the formation of OIBs with unique compositions. The presence of mantle plumes can also influence the melting behavior of the mantle, leading to the formation of different types of OIBs.

Subduction also plays a role in the formation of OIBs. The subduction of oceanic crust can introduce water and other volatile elements into the mantle, which can influence the composition of OIBs. Additionally, the subduction of oceanic crust can also lead to the formation of mantle wedges, which can enhance the melting of the mantle and contribute to the formation of OIBs.

Hotspots, such as the Hawaiian hotspot, also play a role in the formation of OIBs. These hotspots can cause the mantle to rise and melt, leading to the formation of OIBs. The composition of OIBs formed by hotspots can provide valuable insights into the dynamics of the Earth's mantle.

#### 6.1b.3 Geochemistry of OIBs

The geochemistry of OIBs is highly variable, reflecting the diverse processes involved in their formation. OIBs are enriched in trace elements, such as strontium, neodymium, hafnium, lead, and osmium, which can provide insights into the composition of the mantle.

The stable isotope ratios of OIBs also provide valuable information about the mantle. For example, the <sup>87</sup>Sr/<sup>86</sup>Sr ratio can be used to trace the origin of the mantle material, while the <sup>18</sup>O/<sup>16</sup>O ratio can provide insights into the water content of the mantle.

The composition of OIBs can also be used to identify the presence of mantle plumes. For example, the presence of HUIMU, EM1, and EM2 reservoirs can be inferred from the composition of OIBs, providing insights into the dynamics of the Earth's mantle.

In conclusion, the study of OIBs is crucial for understanding the Earth's mantle dynamics. Their diverse compositions and geochemical signatures make them a valuable tool for studying the mantle and its processes.




### Subsection: 6.2a Granitoids

Granitoids are a group of coarse-grained, light-colored igneous rocks that are commonly found in the continents. They are formed by the crystallization of magma at high temperatures and pressures, and they are characterized by their high silica content and their granular texture. Granitoids play a crucial role in the formation of continental crust and are a key component of many igneous rock associations.

#### 6.2a.1 Composition of Granitoids

The composition of granitoids is highly variable, but they are generally characterized by their high silica content. This is due to the fact that granitoids are formed from the crystallization of magma at high temperatures and pressures, which allows for the formation of silicate minerals. The most common minerals found in granitoids include quartz, plagioclase feldspar, and potassium feldspar.

The composition of granitoids can also be influenced by the presence of mantle plumes, which can introduce additional chemical components into the mantle. This can lead to the formation of granitoids with unique compositions, similar to OIBs.

#### 6.2a.2 Formation of Granitoids

The formation of granitoids is a complex process that involves the interaction of various factors, including mantle plumes, subduction, and the presence of hotspots.

Mantle plumes play a crucial role in the formation of granitoids. These plumes can introduce additional chemical components into the mantle, leading to the formation of granitoids with unique compositions. The presence of mantle plumes can also influence the melting behavior of the mantle, leading to the formation of different types of granitoids.

Subduction also plays a role in the formation of granitoids. The subduction of oceanic crust can introduce water and other volatile elements into the mantle, which can influence the composition of granitoids. Additionally, the subduction of oceanic crust can also lead to the formation of mantle wedges, which can enhance the melting of the mantle and contribute to the formation of granitoids.

Hotspots, such as the Hawaiian hotspot, also play a role in the formation of granitoids. The presence of hotspots can lead to the formation of plumes, which can introduce additional chemical components into the mantle and contribute to the formation of granitoids with unique compositions.

#### 6.2a.3 Types of Granitoids

There are several types of granitoids, each with its own unique characteristics. Some of the most common types include:

- **Granite:** This is the most common type of granitoid and is characterized by its light color and coarse-grained texture. It is typically formed from the crystallization of magma at high temperatures and pressures.

- **Diorite:** Diorite is a darker-colored granitoid that is characterized by its medium-grained texture. It is typically formed from the crystallization of magma at lower temperatures and pressures compared to granite.

- **Gabbro:** Gabbro is a dark, fine-grained granitoid that is commonly found in the oceanic crust. It is typically formed from the crystallization of magma at high pressures and temperatures.

- **Tonalite:** Tonalite is a light-colored granitoid that is characterized by its medium-grained texture. It is typically formed from the crystallization of magma at high temperatures and pressures, similar to granite.

- **Monzogranite:** Monzogranite is a light-colored granitoid that is characterized by its fine-grained texture. It is typically formed from the crystallization of magma at high temperatures and pressures, similar to granite.

- **Granodiorite:** Granodiorite is a medium-colored granitoid that is characterized by its medium-grained texture. It is typically formed from the crystallization of magma at high temperatures and pressures, similar to diorite.

- **Plagiogranite:** Plagiogranite is a light-colored granitoid that is characterized by its fine-grained texture. It is typically formed from the crystallization of magma at high temperatures and pressures, similar to tonalite.

- **Teschenite:** Teschenite is a light-colored granitoid that is characterized by its fine-grained texture. It is typically formed from the crystallization of magma at high temperatures and pressures, similar to monzogranite.

- **Svenite:** Svenite is a light-colored granitoid that is characterized by its fine-grained texture. It is typically formed from the crystallization of magma at high temperatures and pressures, similar to granodiorite.

- **Monzogranite Gneiss:** Monzogranite gneiss is a light-colored granitoid that is characterized by its fine-grained texture. It is typically formed from the crystallization of magma at high temperatures and pressures, similar to granite.

- **Granodiorite Gneiss:** Granodiorite gneiss is a medium-colored granitoid that is characterized by its medium-grained texture. It is typically formed from the crystallization of magma at high temperatures and pressures, similar to diorite.

- **Plagiogranite Gneiss:** Plagiogranite gneiss is a light-colored granitoid that is characterized by its fine-grained texture. It is typically formed from the crystallization of magma at high temperatures and pressures, similar to tonalite.

- **Teschenite Gneiss:** Teschenite gneiss is a light-colored granitoid that is characterized by its fine-grained texture. It is typically formed from the crystallization of magma at high temperatures and pressures, similar to monzogranite.

- **Svenite Gneiss:** Svenite gneiss is a light-colored granitoid that is characterized by its fine-grained texture. It is typically formed from the crystallization of magma at high temperatures and pressures, similar to granodiorite.

- **Granophyre Gneiss:** Granophyre gneiss is a light-colored granitoid that is characterized by its fine-grained texture. It is typically formed from the crystallization of magma at high temperatures and pressures, similar to plagiogranite.

- **Granite Gneiss:** Granite gneiss is a light-colored granitoid that is characterized by its medium-grained texture. It is typically formed from the crystallization of magma at high temperatures and pressures, similar to granite.

- **Diorite Gneiss:** Diorite gneiss is a darker-colored granitoid that is characterized by its medium-grained texture. It is typically formed from the crystallization of magma at lower temperatures and pressures compared to granite.

- **Gabbro Gneiss:** Gabbro gneiss is a dark, fine-grained granit


### Subsection: 6.2b Volcanic arcs

Volcanic arcs are a type of igneous rock association that are commonly found in the Pacific Ocean and other areas of volcanic activity. They are formed by the interaction of the Earth's crust and mantle, and are characterized by their linear shape and high volcanic activity.

#### 6.2b.1 Composition of Volcanic Arcs

The composition of volcanic arcs is highly variable, but they are generally characterized by their high silica content and their high iron and magnesium content. This is due to the fact that volcanic arcs are formed from the crystallization of magma at high temperatures and pressures, which allows for the formation of silicate minerals. The most common minerals found in volcanic arcs include quartz, plagioclase feldspar, and potassium feldspar.

The composition of volcanic arcs can also be influenced by the presence of mantle plumes, which can introduce additional chemical components into the mantle. This can lead to the formation of volcanic arcs with unique compositions, similar to OIBs.

#### 6.2b.2 Formation of Volcanic Arcs

The formation of volcanic arcs is a complex process that involves the interaction of various factors, including mantle plumes, subduction, and the presence of hotspots.

Mantle plumes play a crucial role in the formation of volcanic arcs. These plumes can introduce additional chemical components into the mantle, leading to the formation of volcanic arcs with unique compositions. The presence of mantle plumes can also influence the melting behavior of the mantle, leading to the formation of different types of volcanic arcs.

Subduction also plays a role in the formation of volcanic arcs. The subduction of oceanic crust can introduce water and other volatile elements into the mantle, which can influence the composition of volcanic arcs. Additionally, the subduction of oceanic crust can also lead to the formation of mantle wedges, which can enhance the melting behavior of the mantle and contribute to the formation of volcanic arcs.

Hotspots, such as the Hawaiian hotspot, can also play a role in the formation of volcanic arcs. These hotspots can introduce additional heat and melting into the mantle, leading to the formation of volcanic arcs. The presence of hotspots can also influence the direction and intensity of subduction, further impacting the formation of volcanic arcs.

In conclusion, the formation of volcanic arcs is a complex process that involves the interaction of various factors. Mantle plumes, subduction, and hotspots all play a role in the formation of these unique igneous rock associations. By understanding the composition and formation of volcanic arcs, we can gain valuable insights into the processes that shape the Earth's crust and mantle.





### Subsection: 6.3a Continental flood basalts

Continental flood basalts (CFBs) are a type of large igneous province that is characterized by the widespread eruption of basaltic magmas onto the continental crust. These eruptions are typically associated with the formation of extensive lava flows, which can cover areas of hundreds of thousands of square kilometers. CFBs are found on all continents, with the most well-known examples being the Selli Event in the North Atlantic and the Deccan Traps in India.

#### 6.3a.1 Composition of Continental Flood Basalts

The composition of CFBs is highly variable, but they are generally characterized by their high silica content and their high iron and magnesium content. This is due to the fact that CFBs are formed from the crystallization of magma at high temperatures and pressures, which allows for the formation of silicate minerals. The most common minerals found in CFBs include quartz, plagioclase feldspar, and potassium feldspar.

The composition of CFBs can also be influenced by the presence of mantle plumes, which can introduce additional chemical components into the mantle. This can lead to the formation of CFBs with unique compositions, similar to OIBs.

#### 6.3a.2 Formation of Continental Flood Basalts

The formation of CFBs is a complex process that involves the interaction of various factors, including mantle plumes, subduction, and the presence of hotspots.

Mantle plumes play a crucial role in the formation of CFBs. These plumes can introduce additional chemical components into the mantle, leading to the formation of CFBs with unique compositions. The presence of mantle plumes can also influence the melting behavior of the mantle, leading to the formation of different types of CFBs.

Subduction also plays a role in the formation of CFBs. The subduction of oceanic crust can introduce water and other volatile elements into the mantle, which can influence the composition of CFBs. Additionally, the subduction of oceanic crust can also lead to the formation of mantle wedges, which can enhance the melting behavior of the mantle and contribute to the formation of CFBs.

Hotspots, such as the Hawaiian hotspot, can also play a role in the formation of CFBs. The presence of a hotspot can lead to the formation of a mantle plume, which can then contribute to the formation of a CFB. Additionally, the presence of a hotspot can also lead to the formation of a volcanic arc, which can also contribute to the formation of a CFB.

In conclusion, the formation of CFBs is a complex process that involves the interaction of various factors, including mantle plumes, subduction, and hotspots. The composition of CFBs can also be influenced by these factors, leading to the formation of CFBs with unique compositions. Further research is needed to fully understand the formation of CFBs and their role in the Earth's history.





### Subsection: 6.3b Layered mafic intrusions

Layered mafic intrusions are a type of large igneous province that are characterized by the formation of layered mafic rocks. These intrusions are typically found in the oceanic crust and are formed by the differentiation of magma as it cools and solidifies.

#### 6.3b.1 Composition of Layered Mafic Intrusions

The composition of layered mafic intrusions is highly variable, but they are generally characterized by their high iron and magnesium content. This is due to the fact that these intrusions are formed from the crystallization of magma at high temperatures and pressures, which allows for the formation of mafic minerals such as olivine and pyroxene.

The composition of layered mafic intrusions can also be influenced by the presence of mantle plumes, which can introduce additional chemical components into the mantle. This can lead to the formation of intrusions with unique compositions, similar to OIBs.

#### 6.3b.2 Formation of Layered Mafic Intrusions

The formation of layered mafic intrusions is a complex process that involves the interaction of various factors, including mantle plumes, subduction, and the presence of hotspots.

Mantle plumes play a crucial role in the formation of layered mafic intrusions. These plumes can introduce additional chemical components into the mantle, leading to the formation of intrusions with unique compositions. The presence of mantle plumes can also influence the melting behavior of the mantle, leading to the formation of different types of intrusions.

Subduction also plays a role in the formation of layered mafic intrusions. The subduction of oceanic crust can introduce water and other volatile elements into the mantle, which can influence the composition of the intrusions. Additionally, the subduction of oceanic crust can also lead to the formation of layered mafic intrusions as the mantle rises and cools, allowing for the differentiation of magma and the formation of distinct layers.

The presence of hotspots can also contribute to the formation of layered mafic intrusions. Hotspots, such as the Hawaiian hotspot, can introduce additional heat and melting into the mantle, leading to the formation of intrusions with unique compositions. The presence of hotspots can also influence the direction of mantle flow, which can impact the formation of layered mafic intrusions.

In conclusion, the formation of layered mafic intrusions is a complex process that is influenced by various factors, including mantle plumes, subduction, and hotspots. The composition and characteristics of these intrusions can provide valuable insights into the dynamics of the Earth's mantle and the processes that shape our planet.





### Subsection: 6.4a Lunar basalts

Lunar basalts are a type of igneous rock found on the Moon, specifically in the lunar maria or "seas". These rocks are formed by the solidification of basaltic magma, which is a type of mafic (high in iron and magnesium) igneous rock. The composition of lunar basalts is highly variable, but they are generally classified as tholeiitic, with some exceptions such as the KREEP basalts.

#### 6.4a.1 Composition of Lunar Basalts

The composition of lunar basalts is highly variable, but they are generally classified as tholeiitic. This means that they have a high silica content (>52 wt.%) and are relatively low in iron and magnesium. However, there are exceptions to this classification, such as the KREEP basalts, which are abnormally rich in potassium (K), rare-earth elements (REE), and phosphorus (P).

One of the most striking differences between lunar basalts and terrestrial basalts is the near-total absence of water in any form in the lunar basalts. This is due to the lack of hydrogen-bearing minerals like the amphiboles and phyllosilicates that are common in terrestrial basalts.

#### 6.4a.2 Formation of Lunar Basalts

The formation of lunar basalts is a complex process that is still not fully understood. However, it is believed that they are formed by the solidification of basaltic magma, which is generated by the melting of the Moon's mantle. The Moon's mantle is believed to be composed of a mixture of olivine, pyroxene, and plagioclase feldspar, which are the primary minerals found in lunar basalts.

The Moon's mantle is believed to be composed of a mixture of olivine, pyroxene, and plagioclase feldspar, which are the primary minerals found in lunar basalts. The melting of the mantle is believed to be caused by the Moon's internal heat, which is generated by the decay of radioactive elements and residual heat from its formation.

The Moon's mantle is also believed to be composed of a mixture of olivine, pyroxene, and plagioclase feldspar, which are the primary minerals found in lunar basalts. The melting of the mantle is believed to be caused by the Moon's internal heat, which is generated by the decay of radioactive elements and residual heat from its formation.

#### 6.4a.3 Types of Lunar Basalts

There are three main types of lunar basalts: high-Ti basalts, low-Ti basalts, and very-low-Ti (VLT) basalts. These basalts are classified based on their major element chemistry, specifically their titanium (Ti) content. High-Ti basalts have the highest TiO<sub>2</sub> abundances, reaching up to 15 wt.%, while low-Ti basalts have lower TiO<sub>2</sub> abundances, typically less than 4 wt.%. Very-low-Ti (VLT) basalts have the lowest TiO<sub>2</sub> abundances, typically less than 1 wt.%.

The high-Ti basalts were once thought to be distinct from the low-Ti and VLT basalts, but recent remote sensing data from the Clementine mission has shown that there is a continuum of titanium concentrations between these end members. This has led to the reclassification of some high-Ti basalts as low-Ti basalts.

#### 6.4a.4 KREEP Basalts

The KREEP basalts are a special group of lunar basalts that are abnormally rich in potassium (K), rare-earth elements (REE), and phosphorus (P). These basalts are believed to have formed from the melting of a deep mantle reservoir, known as the KREEP layer, which is composed of potassium-rich pyroxene. The KREEP layer is believed to have formed early in the Moon's history, and its melting is believed to have contributed to the formation of the Moon's early crust.

The KREEP basalts are believed to have formed from the melting of a deep mantle reservoir, known as the KREEP layer, which is composed of potassium-rich pyroxene. This layer is believed to have formed early in the Moon's history, and its melting is believed to have contributed to the formation of the Moon's early crust.

The KREEP basalts are named for their high potassium (K), rare-earth elements (REE), and phosphorus (P) content, which is believed to have come from the melting of the KREEP layer. These basalts are believed to have formed early in the Moon's history, and their presence is believed to have contributed to the formation of the Moon's early crust.




### Subsection: 6.4b Martian basalts

Martian basalts are a type of igneous rock found on the surface of Mars. They are formed by the solidification of basaltic magma, which is a type of mafic (high in iron and magnesium) igneous rock. The composition of Martian basalts is highly variable, but they are generally classified as tholeiitic, with some exceptions such as the SNC meteorites.

#### 6.4b.1 Composition of Martian Basalts

The composition of Martian basalts is highly variable, but they are generally classified as tholeiitic. This means that they have a high silica content (>52 wt.%) and are relatively low in iron and magnesium. However, there are exceptions to this classification, such as the SNC meteorites, which are abnormally rich in potassium (K), rare-earth elements (REE), and phosphorus (P).

One of the most striking differences between Martian basalts and terrestrial basalts is the near-total absence of water in any form in the Martian basalts. This is due to the lack of hydrogen-bearing minerals like the amphiboles and phyllosilicates that are common in terrestrial basalts.

#### 6.4b.2 Formation of Martian Basalts

The formation of Martian basalts is a complex process that is still not fully understood. However, it is believed that they are formed by the solidification of basaltic magma, which is generated by the melting of the Martian mantle. The Martian mantle is believed to be composed of a mixture of olivine, pyroxene, and plagioclase feldspar, which are the primary minerals found in Martian basalts.

The Martian mantle is believed to be composed of a mixture of olivine, pyroxene, and plagioclase feldspar, which are the primary minerals found in Martian basalts. The melting of the mantle is believed to be caused by the Martian internal heat, which is generated by the decay of radioactive elements and residual heat from the formation of the planet.

The Martian mantle is also believed to be composed of a mixture of olivine, pyroxene, and plagioclase feldspar, which are the primary minerals found in Martian basalts. The melting of the mantle is believed to be caused by the Martian internal heat, which is generated by the decay of radioactive elements and residual heat from the formation of the planet.

#### 6.4b.3 Martian Basalt Associations

Martian basalts are found in various associations on the surface of Mars. These associations are formed by the interaction of different types of basalts and other igneous rocks. The most common associations are the Columbia Hills, which are composed of a variety of rocks, some of which have been altered by water, but not by very much water. The dust in Gusev Crater is the same as dust all around the planet and is magnetic. The spectra of the dust was similar to spectra of bright, low thermal inertia regions like Tharsis and Arabia that have been detected by orbiting satellites. A thin layer of dust, maybe less than one millimeter thick covers all surfaces. Something in it contains a small amount of chemically bound water.

### Conclusion

In this section, we have explored the composition and formation of Martian basalts. We have learned that they are primarily composed of olivine, pyroxene, and plagioclase feldspar, and are classified as tholeiitic. The formation of Martian basalts is a complex process that is still not fully understood, but it is believed to be caused by the melting of the Martian mantle. We have also discussed the various associations of Martian basalts, including the Columbia Hills and the dust in Gusev Crater. These associations provide valuable insights into the geological history of Mars and the processes that have shaped its surface.


### Conclusion
In this chapter, we have explored the various types of igneous rock associations found on Earth. We have learned about the different processes that contribute to the formation of these associations, such as fractional melting, magma mixing, and crystal settling. We have also discussed the importance of understanding these associations in order to decipher the geological history of a region.

One of the key takeaways from this chapter is the concept of fractional melting. This process plays a crucial role in the formation of igneous rock associations, as it allows for the separation of different minerals and the creation of distinct rock types. By studying the composition and texture of different rock types, we can gain insight into the conditions under which they were formed.

Another important aspect of igneous rock associations is magma mixing. This process occurs when different types of magma interact with each other, resulting in the formation of new rock types. By studying the composition and texture of these mixed magmas, we can better understand the evolution of magmatic systems and the factors that influence their behavior.

Finally, we have discussed the role of crystal settling in the formation of igneous rock associations. This process is responsible for the layering of different minerals within a rock, providing valuable information about the cooling and solidification of magma. By studying these layers, we can reconstruct the conditions under which the magma was formed and the processes that it underwent.

In conclusion, the study of igneous rock associations is crucial for understanding the geological history of a region. By examining the composition, texture, and structure of these rock types, we can gain insight into the processes that have shaped the Earth's crust and the factors that influence the formation of igneous rocks.

### Introduction

In this chapter, we will delve into the fascinating world of meteorite petrology. Meteorites are fragments of rock or metal that have fallen to Earth from outer space. They provide valuable insights into the composition and processes of the early solar system, as well as the formation and evolution of the Earth. By studying the petrology of meteorites, we can gain a better understanding of the origins of our planet and its place in the universe.

We will begin by exploring the different types of meteorites, including iron, stony-iron, and stony meteorites. Each type has its own unique characteristics and can provide valuable information about the conditions and processes that existed in the early solar system. We will also discuss the formation of meteorites and how they are classified based on their composition and structure.

Next, we will delve into the petrology of meteorites, examining their mineral and chemical composition. We will learn about the different types of minerals found in meteorites and how they can be used to determine the origin and history of a meteorite. We will also explore the various processes that can alter the composition of meteorites, such as melting, crystallization, and metamorphism.

Finally, we will discuss the role of meteorites in the study of the Earth's early history. By analyzing the composition and structure of meteorites, we can gain insights into the conditions and processes that existed on Earth billions of years ago. We will also explore the potential for life on other planets and the role of meteorites in the search for extraterrestrial life.

Join us on this journey through the world of meteorite petrology and discover the wonders of our universe.
